Why Confusion Arises: Shared Accidents and Names
Taftazani explains the cause of confusion (ishbah) regarding species and the comparison of color and light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why people mistakenly think that general groups of species are a single species, and how the case of light illustrates a different confusion.

Core Idea
Shared accidents and names cause confusion, making groups appear as one type.

Taftazani now explains the source of confusion (ishbah): some groups of species share a common accident and a common name, leading one to imagine that the whole group is a single species. For example, all whites share the accident of dispersing vision and the name 'whiteness'. This lesson unpacks that confusion and contrasts it with the case of light.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on confusion
Focus on the part beginning with 'وإنما يقع الاشتباه'. Understand the mechanism of shared accident and shared name.
2
Identify the examples
Notice the examples: whites share the accident of dispersing vision and name 'whiteness'; blacks share contracting vision and name 'blackness'. Then compare with lights, which lack such grouping.
3
Understand the case of light and color
Taftazani then compares color and light. Color is a genus, while light is mistakenly thought to be a type due to closeness of its types. See how the confusion differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهر من كلام القوم أن أنواع اللون هي السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك، وأنواع الكيفيات الملموسة هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، ونحو ذلك، إلا أن التحقيق هو أن أنواع اللون هي البياضات المخصوصة التي لا تتفاوت أفرادها كبياض الثلج مثلا، وكذا في السواد وغيره، بل في كل ما يقال بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات ليس مطلق الحرارة بل الحرارة المخصوصة التي تكون في أفرادها على السوية كحرارة النار الصرفة مثلاً، والنوع من المبصرات ليس مطلق الضوء، بل الضوء الخاص الذي لا يتفاوت فيه أفراده كضوء الشمس مثلاً، وإنما يقع الاشتباه من جهة أن الأنواع قد يكون لجملة جملة منها عارض خاص، واسم خاص كالبياضات المشتركة في تفريق البصر وفي اسم البياض والسوادات المشتركة في قبض البصر، وفي اسم السواد وكالحرارات والبرودات، ونحو ذلك فيتوهم أن تلك الجملة نوع واحد بخلاف الأضواء، فإنه لا ينفرد جملة جملة منها بعارض واسم فلا يتوهم ذلك فيه، بل ربما يتوهم كون المجموع نوعاً واحداً فاللون والضوء قد وقعا في مرتبة واحدة من المبصرات، إلا أن اللون جنس الألوان بخلاف الضوء لما فيه من التفاوت، والضوء توهم نوعية لتقارب أنواعه بخلاف اللون، وإنما توهم ذلك في جملة من أنواعه كالبياض مثلاً لتقارب أنواع البياض، وكالسواد لتقارب أنواع السواد وعلى هذا القياس فصار الضوء بمنزلة البياض مثلاً في أنه ليس نوعاً لما تحته ولا جنساً بل عارضاً، ومبنى ذلك على ما تقرر عندهم من أن القول بالتشكيك لا يكون إلا عارضاً لامتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، لأن الأمر الذي به يتحقق التفاوت حيث يوجد في الأشد دون الأضعف إن لم يكن داخلا في الماهية لم يتحقق التفاوت فيها بل كانت في الكل على السواء، وإن كان داخلا فيها لم يتحقق اشتراك الأضعف فيها لانتفاء بعض الأجزاء مثلاً الخصوصية التي توجد في نور الشمس دون القمر، إن كانت من ذاتيات الضوء لم يكن ما في القمر ضوء، وإلا لم يكن تفاوت النورين في نفس الماهية.
The apparent from the speech of the scholars is that the types of color are black, white, red, yellow, and others, and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and so forth. However, the verified view is that the types of color are the specific whites whose individuals do not differ, like the whiteness of snow for example, and similarly in blackness and others, rather in everything that is said by gradation (tashkik) such that the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but the specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire for example, and the type of visual things is not absolute light but the specific light whose individuals do not differ, like the light of the sun for example. And confusion only arises from the fact that some groups of types may have a specific accident and a specific name, like the whites sharing in dispersing the sight and in the name 'whiteness', and the blacks sharing in contracting the sight and in the name 'blackness', and likewise heats and colds, and so on, so one imagines that that group is one type, unlike lights, for no group of them has a distinct accident and name, so that is not imagined of them, rather one might imagine that the whole collection is one type. So color and light are in the same rank among visual things, except that color is a genus of colors unlike light because of the difference in it, and light [people] imagined to be a type due to the closeness of its types unlike color, and that was only imagined of a group of its types like whiteness for example due to the closeness of types of whiteness, and like blackness due to the closeness of types of blackness, and analogously, light became like whiteness for example in that it is neither a type for what is under it nor a genus but rather an accident. And the basis of this is what is established with them: that the claim of gradation (tashkik) is only accidental because of the impossibility of difference in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat). For the thing by which difference is realized, when it exists in the more intense (ashadd) without the weaker (ad`af), if it is not included in the essence, difference is not realized in it, rather it is the same in all; and if it is included in it, the weaker does not share in it due to the absence of some parts. For example, the specificity that exists in the light of the sun but not the moon: if it were among the essentials of light, what is in the moon would not be light; otherwise, the difference between the two lights would not be in the essence itself.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and argument about the true nature of species (anwa') of qualities like colors and tactile properties. It begins by stating the apparent view of scholars: that the types of color are general categories like black, white, red, yellow, etc., and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness. Then it presents the verified view (tahqiq): the true types are the specific individuals that do not differ among themselves, for example, specific whites like the whiteness of snow, and similarly for blackness and others. This applies to everything said by gradation (tashkik). So the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire; the type of visual things is not absolute light but specific light whose individuals are equal, like sunlight. The passage then explains the cause of confusion (ishbah): some groups of species share a common accident and name, e.g., whites share the accident of dispersing vision and the name 'whiteness', blacks share contracting vision and the name 'blackness', heats share the accident of heat, etc. This leads one to mistakenly think that group is one single species. In contrast, lights do not have such a common accident and name per group, so that confusion does not arise; instead, one might imagine all lights are one type. Then it compares color and light: both are in the same rank of visual things, but color is a genus of colors, while light is not a genus due to difference within it; light is mistakenly thought to be a type because its types are close, but that mistake only occurs for groups like whiteness due to closeness. Accordingly, light is like whiteness: neither a type for what is under it nor a genus, but an accident. The basis (mabna) of all this is the established principle: gradation (tashkik) is only accidental because difference cannot occur in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat). The reasoning: if the factor of difference exists in the more intense but not the weaker, and it is not part of the essence, then there is no difference in the essence itself—it is the same in all; if it is part of the essence, then the weaker does not share in that essence, because part of the essence is missing. For example, the specificity that makes sunlight different from moonlight: if that specificity were essential to light, then moonlight would not be light; if not, then the difference between the two lights is not in the essence of light itself. Hence, gradation is only accidental.

Key Terms
اشتباه (ishbah)
عارض (`arad)
جنس (jins)
نوع (naw`)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a group of species share an accident and name, they are a single species.
Correction: The shared accident and name are not sufficient; the true species are the specific individuals. The apparent grouping is due to accidental commonality.

Checkpoint
What causes people to mistakenly think that a group like all whites is a single species?
A) The individuals are all the same
B) They share a common accident and a common name
C) The group has no internal difference
D) The group is a genus

Key Takeaway
Shared accidents and names create the illusion of a single species, but true species are the specific individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you lumped a diverse group of people or ideas under a single label just because they shared a common name or surface-level trait? How did that confusion affect your judgment, and what would it take for you to see the true variety within?
?
In your study of Islamic theology, have you ever assumed that because two arguments share a term (like 'attribute' or 'essence') they must be the same doctrine? How can you use Taftazani's warning to sharpen your discernment?
?
Reflect on a time when you were convinced that a certain group (e.g., a school of thought, a community) was monolithic. What shared 'accident' or 'name' misled you? Commit to actively seeking out the real diversity within that group.

Logic and Technical Terms

The Principle of Gradation: Why Difference Cannot Be in the Essence
Taftazani establishes the principle that gradation is accidental due to impossibility of difference in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that gradation (tashkik) can only be accidental, not essential, because difference cannot occur in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat).

Core Idea
Gradation is only accidental; difference in essence is impossible.

Taftazani concludes the passage by grounding the previous discussion in a fundamental logical principle: the claim of gradation (tashkik) can only be accidental. He argues that difference cannot occur in the essence or its essentials, using a conditional argument. This lesson explains that principle and its application to the example of sunlight and moonlight.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Start from 'ومبنى ذلك على ما تقرر عندهم' and read to the end. Understand the core argument about tashkik.
2
Analyze the conditional reasoning
The passage presents two conditionals: if the factor of difference is not inside the essence, then no difference; if it is inside, then the weaker lacks part of the essence. Grasp both scenarios.
3
Apply to the sunlight/moonlight example
See how the example illustrates the principle: the specificity of sunlight cannot be essential, otherwise moonlight wouldn't be light; if not essential, the difference is not in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهر من كلام القوم أن أنواع اللون هي السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك، وأنواع الكيفيات الملموسة هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، ونحو ذلك، إلا أن التحقيق هو أن أنواع اللون هي البياضات المخصوصة التي لا تتفاوت أفرادها كبياض الثلج مثلا، وكذا في السواد وغيره، بل في كل ما يقال بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات ليس مطلق الحرارة بل الحرارة المخصوصة التي تكون في أفرادها على السوية كحرارة النار الصرفة مثلاً، والنوع من المبصرات ليس مطلق الضوء، بل الضوء الخاص الذي لا يتفاوت فيه أفراده كضوء الشمس مثلاً، وإنما يقع الاشتباه من جهة أن الأنواع قد يكون لجملة جملة منها عارض خاص، واسم خاص كالبياضات المشتركة في تفريق البصر وفي اسم البياض والسوادات المشتركة في قبض البصر، وفي اسم السواد وكالحرارات والبرودات، ونحو ذلك فيتوهم أن تلك الجملة نوع واحد بخلاف الأضواء، فإنه لا ينفرد جملة جملة منها بعارض واسم فلا يتوهم ذلك فيه، بل ربما يتوهم كون المجموع نوعاً واحداً فاللون والضوء قد وقعا في مرتبة واحدة من المبصرات، إلا أن اللون جنس الألوان بخلاف الضوء لما فيه من التفاوت، والضوء توهم نوعية لتقارب أنواعه بخلاف اللون، وإنما توهم ذلك في جملة من أنواعه كالبياض مثلاً لتقارب أنواع البياض، وكالسواد لتقارب أنواع السواد وعلى هذا القياس فصار الضوء بمنزلة البياض مثلاً في أنه ليس نوعاً لما تحته ولا جنساً بل عارضاً، ومبنى ذلك على ما تقرر عندهم من أن القول بالتشكيك لا يكون إلا عارضاً لامتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، لأن الأمر الذي به يتحقق التفاوت حيث يوجد في الأشد دون الأضعف إن لم يكن داخلا في الماهية لم يتحقق التفاوت فيها بل كانت في الكل على السواء، وإن كان داخلا فيها لم يتحقق اشتراك الأضعف فيها لانتفاء بعض الأجزاء مثلاً الخصوصية التي توجد في نور الشمس دون القمر، إن كانت من ذاتيات الضوء لم يكن ما في القمر ضوء، وإلا لم يكن تفاوت النورين في نفس الماهية.
The apparent from the speech of the scholars is that the types of color are black, white, red, yellow, and others, and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and so forth. However, the verified view is that the types of color are the specific whites whose individuals do not differ, like the whiteness of snow for example, and similarly in blackness and others, rather in everything that is said by gradation (tashkik) such that the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but the specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire for example, and the type of visual things is not absolute light but the specific light whose individuals do not differ, like the light of the sun for example. And confusion only arises from the fact that some groups of types may have a specific accident and a specific name, like the whites sharing in dispersing the sight and in the name 'whiteness', and the blacks sharing in contracting the sight and in the name 'blackness', and likewise heats and colds, and so on, so one imagines that that group is one type, unlike lights, for no group of them has a distinct accident and name, so that is not imagined of them, rather one might imagine that the whole collection is one type. So color and light are in the same rank among visual things, except that color is a genus of colors unlike light because of the difference in it, and light [people] imagined to be a type due to the closeness of its types unlike color, and that was only imagined of a group of its types like whiteness for example due to the closeness of types of whiteness, and like blackness due to the closeness of types of blackness, and analogously, light became like whiteness for example in that it is neither a type for what is under it nor a genus but rather an accident. And the basis of this is what is established with them: that the claim of gradation (tashkik) is only accidental because of the impossibility of difference in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat). For the thing by which difference is realized, when it exists in the more intense (ashadd) without the weaker (ad`af), if it is not included in the essence, difference is not realized in it, rather it is the same in all; and if it is included in it, the weaker does not share in it due to the absence of some parts. For example, the specificity that exists in the light of the sun but not the moon: if it were among the essentials of light, what is in the moon would not be light; otherwise, the difference between the two lights would not be in the essence itself.

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and argument about the true nature of species (anwa') of qualities like colors and tactile properties. This applies to everything said by gradation (tashkik). So the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire; the type of visual things is not absolute light but specific light whose individuals are equal, like sunlight. The passage then explains the cause of confusion (ishbah): some groups of species share a common accident and name, e.g., whites share the accident of dispersing vision and the name 'whiteness', blacks share contracting vision and the name 'blackness', heats share the accident of heat, etc. This leads one to mistakenly think that group is one single species. In contrast, lights do not have such a common accident and name per group, so that confusion does not arise; instead, one might imagine all lights are one type. Then it compares color and light: both are in the same rank of visual things, but color is a genus of colors, while light is not a genus due to difference within it; light is mistakenly thought to be a type because its types are close, but that mistake only occurs for groups like whiteness due to closeness. The reasoning: if the factor of difference exists in the more intense but not the weaker, and it is not part of the essence, then there is no difference in the essence itself—it is the same in all; if it is part of the essence, then the weaker does not share in that essence, because part of the essence is missing. Hence, gradation is only accidental.

Key Terms
تشكيك (tashkik)
ماهية (mahiyya)
ذاتيات (dhatiyyat)
عارض (`arad)
أشدّ (ashadd)
أضعف (ad`af)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Gradation (tashkik) can be essential; some things differ in their very essence by degree.
Correction: The passage proves that difference in essence is impossible; any apparent difference in intensity is accidental, not essential.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion about the difference between sunlight and moonlight?
A) It is an essential difference in the essence of light
B) It is not in the essence of light but accidental
C) Moonlight is not light
D) Sunlight and moonlight are the same species

Key Takeaway
Gradation can only be accidental because essential difference would undermine the very nature of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that difference between the more and less intense is merely accidental, not essential. When you see a Muslim with weaker faith than yours, do you see them as sharing the same essential faith or as somehow less of a believer? Will you commit to treating them as equal in essence, even if their practice differs?
?
The example of sunlight and moonlight: both share the same essence of light despite difference in intensity. In your own life, do you sometimes feel inferior or superior to others based on accidental qualities like wealth, knowledge, or status? How does this lesson challenge your view of yourself and others before Allah?

Logic and Technical Terms

Light as Accident: The Principle of Tashkīk
Al-Taftazani argues that light is an accident, not a genus or species, using the principle of tashkīk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why light is classified as an accident ('āriḍ) rather than a genus or species, based on the principle that tashkīk (systematic ambiguity) applies only to accidents.

Core Idea
Light is not a genus or species because its variations are accidental, not essential.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqāṣid tackles a subtle logical problem: why light, despite having different intensities (like sunlight vs. moonlight), is not considered a genus or species but an accident. The author, al-Taftazāni, uses the concept of tashkīk to show that essential quiddities cannot have internal differences—therefore, any variation must be accidental. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the examples (whitenesses, blacknesses, etc.) and the comparison with color.
2
Identify the key comparison
The author compares light to accidental categories like whiteness and blackness. Note how these categories have subgroups that share a name and property, leading to a mistaken impression of being a species. Ask yourself: how is light similar and different?
3
Understand the underlying principle
The principle of tashkīk is introduced at the end. Why can't quiddities have internal differences? Reread the argument about the stronger and weaker. This is the core logical move that proves light is an accident.
The example of sunlight vs. moonlight is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضوء الخاص الذي لا يتفاوت فيه أفراده كضوء الشمس مثلاً، وإنما يقع الاشتباه من جهة أن الأنواع قد يكون لجملة جملة منها عارض خاص، واسم خاص كالبياضات المشتركة في تفريق البصر وفي اسم البياض والسوادات المشتركة في قبض البصر، وفي اسم السواد وكالحرارات والبرودات، ونحو ذلك فيتوهم أن تلك الجملة نوع واحد بخلاف الأضواء، فإنه لا ينفرد جملة جملة منها بعارض واسم فلا يتوهم ذلك فيه، بل ربما يتوهم كون المجموع نوعاً واحداً فاللون والضوء قد وقعا في مرتبة واحدة من المبصرات، إلا أن اللون جنس الألوان بخلاف الضوء لما فيه من التفاوت والضوء توهم نوعية لتقارب أنواعه بخلاف اللون، وإنما توهم ذلك في جملة من أنواعه كالبياض مثلاً لتقارب أنواع البياض، وكالسواد لتقارب أنواع السواد وعلى هذا القياس فصار الضوء بمنزلة البياض مثلاً في أنه ليس نوعاً لما تحته ولا جنساً بل عارضاً، ومبنى ذلك على ما تقرر عندهم من أن القول بالتشكيك لا يكون إلا عارضاً لامتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، لأن الأمر الذي به يتحقق التفاوت حيث يوجد في الأشد دون الأضعف إن لم يكن داخلا في الماهية لم يتحقق التفاوت فيها بل كانت في الكل على السواء، وإن كان داخلا فيها لم يتحقق اشتراك الأضعف فيها لانتفاء بعض الأجزاء مثلاً الخصوصية التي توجد في نور الشمس دون القمر، إن كانت من ذاتيات الضوء لم يكن ما في القمر ضوء، وإلا لم يكن تفاوت النورين في نفس الماهية.
The particular light whose individuals do not differ, like sunlight for example, but the confusion arises from the fact that species sometimes have a particular accident and particular name for each group of them, like the whitenesses that share in dispersing sight and in the name of whiteness, and the blacknesses that share in contracting sight and in the name of blackness, and like heats and colds, and the like; so one imagines that group to be one species, unlike the lights, for no group of them is singled out by an accident and a name, so one does not imagine that in them; rather one might imagine the whole to be one species. So color and light have fallen into the same rank among visible things, except that color is a genus of colors, unlike light because of the difference in it. And light: one imagines it to be a species due to the closeness of its types, unlike color; rather one imagines that in a group of its types like whiteness, for example, due to the closeness of the types of whiteness, and like blackness due to the closeness of the types of blackness. And on this analogy, light became like whiteness, for example, in that it is not a species for what is under it nor a genus, but an accident. And the basis of that is what is established with them: that the statement of tashkik is only an accident, because difference in quiddity and its essentials is impossible. For the matter by which the difference is realized, when it exists in the stronger without the weaker, if it is not internal to the quiddity, then the difference is not realized in it, rather it is uniform in all; and if it is internal to it, then the sharing of the weaker in it is not realized due to the absence of some parts. For example: the particularity that exists in the light of the sun but not the moon: if it is among the essentials of light, then what is in the moon is not light; otherwise, the two lights do not differ in the quiddity itself.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why light is considered an accident ('āriḍ) rather than a genus (jins) or species (naw'). The author begins by observing that the individuals of 'particular light' (al-ḍaw' al-khāṣṣ), such as sunlight, do not differ from each other in kind. Confusion arises when we consider other categories: for example, different shades of whiteness share both a property (dispersing sight) and a common name, leading one to mistakenly think they form a single species. Similarly for blackness, heat, cold, etc. But light is different: its subgroups do not have unique accidental properties or separate names, so we do not mistakenly call each subgroup a species; rather, we might mistakenly think all light is one species. The author then compares light and color: both are visible, but color is a genus of many species, while light is not. Why? Because light's types are so similar that we imagine it to be a species, whereas color's types are more varied. Yet, just as whiteness (e.g., snow white vs. ivory white) is not a genus or species but an accident despite the closeness of its types, so too is light. The underlying principle (mabnā) is that 'tashkik' (systematic ambiguity, where a term applies to different instances with varying intensity) only applies to accidents, not to quiddities (māhiyāt) or their essentials. This is because the factor that causes difference—if it exists in the stronger but not the weaker—cannot be part of the quiddity; if it were outside the quiddity, the quiddity would be uniform across all instances; if it were inside, the weaker instance would lack part of the quiddity and thus not share the same essence. The example of sunlight versus moonlight illustrates: if the additional quality that makes sunlight brighter were part of the essence of light, then moonlight would not be light; if it were not part of the essence, then both are equally light in essence, and the difference is merely accidental. Therefore, light is an accident, not a genus or species.

Key Terms

التشكيك

الماهية
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because light has different intensities (e.g., sunlight vs. moonlight), it must be a species with varying degrees, or even a genus containing multiple species.
Correction: The intensity difference is accidental, not essential. If it were essential, moonlight would not be light. Therefore, light is a single accident, not a genus or species.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is light not considered a genus or species?
A) Because its individuals are identical without any difference.
B) Because it has no specific name unlike colors.
C) Because it is an accident ('āriḍ) and not a quiddity (māhiyya).
D) Because it is a color and color is a genus.

Key Takeaway
Light is an accident because its quiddity is uniform across instances; differences in intensity are accidental, proving that tashkīk applies only to accidental categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that what we perceive as difference in intensity (like sunlight vs moonlight) does not change the essence of light. In your life, do you sometimes treat your own or others' worth as dependent on accidental qualities (wealth, status, mood) rather than the essential human dignity of being created by Allah? How can you realign your view today?
?
If tashkīk teaches us that only accidents can have gradation, then what does it mean that Allah's attributes (like mercy or power) are described in degrees in the Qur'an? Does this challenge you to think of His attributes as utterly transcendent—beyond accidental variation? What does that demand of your reverence?

Defending Tashkik in Accidents
The author addresses an objection that tashkik would be impossible in accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility of tashkik (systematic ambiguity) in accidents against an objection that would make it impossible.

Core Idea
Tashkik in accidents is possible because the extra quality belongs to the subject, not the accident.

A dialectical objection claims that if quiddities cannot differ, then accidents cannot either—because the extra quality must be part of the accident's concept or not. The author answers by distinguishing between the accident's concept and the subject's quiddity, showing that the extra quality can be internal to the subject without affecting the accident's uniformity. Then a further challenge arises, and the author refines the argument. This passage sharpens your understanding of tashkik and difference in philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection's logic: if the earlier proof (that quiddities do not admit tashkik) holds, then it would also apply to accidents. The extra quality must either be part of the accident's concept or not, both leading to problems.
The example: whiteness in snow vs. ivory.
2
Analyze the answer
Notice the author's response: the additional amount is internal to the quiddity of the stronger subject, not to the accident itself. This allows the accident to remain conceptually uniform while differing by subject.
Key distinction: accident concept vs. subject quiddity.
3
Follow the counter-objection
A hypothetical objector applies the same reasoning to quiddities: if an external factor can cause difference in accidents, why not in quiddities? For example, light as genus and the particularity in sunlight.
This challenges the original proof's asymmetry.
4
Examine the final response
The author directs the prevention: we do not grant that an external additional amount necessarily leaves the quiddity uniform; it only would if the addition is not of the same kind. If it is of the same kind (e.g., more light), it can increase the accident and also apply to quiddities.
The example of increments in whiteness, heat, etc.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion (al-ḥāṣil) states that if non-inclusion of the additional amount in the common meaning prevented difference, then no concept could have differing instances—that is the meaning of the counter-argument (naqḍ). Thus, the original proof stands for quiddities but not for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو صح هذا الدليل لزم أن لا يكون العارض أيضاً مقولاً بالتشكيك قابلاً للشدة والضعف لأن القدر الزائد إما داخل في مفهوم العارض فيهما على السواء، مثلا الخصوصية التي توجد في بياض الثلج دون العاج، إن كانت مأخوذة في مفهوم البياض لم يكن ما في العج من معروضاته، وإلا لكان مفهوم البياض فيهما على السواء. أجيب بأنه داخل في ماهية المعروض الأشد، وإن لم يدخل في ماهية العارض ولا في ماهية المعروض الأضعف، ولا يلزم من عدم دخوله في مفهوم العارض تساويه في جميع المعروضات ولقائل أن يقول : فيتوجه مثله على الدليل المذكور على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذلك لأنه لما جاز التفاوت في العارض باعتبار أمر خارج عنه داخل في ماهية بعض المعروضات فلم لا يجوز في الماهية باعتبار أمر خارج عنها داخل في هوية بعض الأفراد، مثلاً يكون النور تمام ماهية الأنوار أو جنساً لها وتكون الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس أمراً خارجاً عن حقيقة النور، داخلاً في هوية نور الشمس على هذا القياس . وتوجيه المنع أنا لا نسلم أن القدر الزائد إن كان خارجاً عن الماهية كانت الماهية في الكل على السواء، وإنما يلزم لو لم يكن ذلك زيادة من جنس الماهية ، وإذا تحققت فلا عبرة بكونه داخلا في ماهية المعروض حتى لو فرضنا الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس من عوارضه كان التفاوت بحاله، وإنما العبرة بكونه من جنس العارض وزيادة فيه، فإن الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس، وبياض الثلج وحرارة النار ليست إلا زيادة نور وبياض، وحرارة، ولا يمتنع مثل ذلك في الماهية وذاتياتها . والحاصل : أن عدم دخول القدر الزائد الذي به التفاوت في المعنى المشترك الذي فيه التفاوت، إن كان مانعاً من التفاوت لزم عدم تفاوت شيء من المفهومات في أفراده سواء كان عارضاً لهما، أو ذاتياً، وهو معنى النقض.
For if it is said: If this proof were valid, it would follow that the accident also is not said by tashkik, capable of intensity and weakness, because the additional amount is either internal to the concept of the accident in both equally; for example, the particularity that exists in the whiteness of snow but not in ivory: if it is taken into the concept of whiteness, then what is in ivory would not be among its subjects; otherwise, the concept of whiteness would be the same in both. It is answered that it is internal to the quiddity of the stronger subject, although it is not internal to the quiddity of the accident nor to the quiddity of the weaker subject, and it does not follow from its being not internal to the concept of the accident that it is equal in all subjects. And someone may say: Then the like of it applies to the mentioned proof on the impossibility of difference in quiddity, because when difference is allowed in the accident by virtue of a matter external to it, internal to the quiddity of some subjects, then why is it not allowed in quiddity by virtue of a matter external to it, internal in the identity (huwiyya) of some individuals? For example: light might be the complete quiddity of lights or a genus for them, and the particularity that is in sunlight might be a matter external to the reality of light, internal in the identity of sunlight on this analogy. And the direction of the prevention (tawjīh al-man') is that we do not grant that if the additional amount is external to the quiddity, then the quiddity is the same in all; rather it would follow only if that addition were not of the same kind as the quiddity. And if it is realized, then no consideration is given to its being internal to the quiddity of the subject; even if we assume that the particularity in sunlight is among its accidents, the difference would remain as it is. Rather, the consideration is that it is of the same kind as the accident and an increase in it. For the particularity in sunlight, and in the whiteness of snow, and the heat of fire, is nothing but an increase of light, whiteness, and heat; and the like of that is not impossible in quiddity and its essentials. And the conclusion: that the non-inclusion of the additional amount by which the difference occurs in the common meaning in which the difference occurs, if it were a preventer of difference, would entail that no concept differs in its individuals, whether it is an accident for them or essential; and that is the meaning of the counter-example (naqḍ).
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer, followed by a further dialectical exchange and a final summary. The objection states: If the earlier proof (that difference in quiddity is impossible) is correct, then it would also follow that an accident (al-'āriḍ) cannot be said by tashkik (systematic ambiguity) with degrees of intensity and weakness. Why? Because the additional amount (al-qadar al-zā'id) that causes the difference would have to be either part of the concept of the accident in both the strong and weak instances or not. Using the example of whiteness in snow versus ivory: if the extra whiteness of snow is part of the concept of whiteness, then ivory would not be a subject of whiteness; if not, then the concept is the same in both, so no real intensity. Thus the proof would apply to accidents too, contradicting the view that accidents admit tashkik. The answer (ajīb) explains that the additional amount is internal to the quiddity of the stronger subject (ma'riḍ al-ashadd), not to the accident itself nor to the weaker subject. Therefore, the accident's concept remains uniform, and the difference arises from the subject. This answer is then challenged by a hypothetical objector (li-qā'il an yaqūl): if difference in accidents can come from something external to the accident but internal to the subject, why can't the same apply to quiddities? For example, light could be a genus, and the extra quality in sunlight could be external to light's essence but internal to the identity (huwiyya) of sunlight. This would allow difference in quiddity as well. The author responds by directing the prevention (tawjīh al-man'), stating that we do not grant that an external additional amount necessarily leaves the quiddity uniform; that would only follow if the addition were not of the same kind (jins) as the quiddity. If the addition is of the same kind (like more light, more whiteness, more heat), then it is an increase in that accident itself, and it can occur in the quiddity and its essentials without contradiction. The conclusion (al-ḥāṣil) is that if the non-inclusion of the additional amount in the common meaning were a preventer of difference, then no concept could have differing instances—whether the concept is an accident or an essential—which is the meaning of the counter-argument (naqḍ). Thus, the original proof stands: quiddities do not admit tashkik, but accidents do because their differences come from their subjects or from additional increments of the same kind.

التشكيك

المعروض
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if accidents admit tashkik, then quiddities should too, because the same logic applies.
Correction: The distinction is that tashkik in accidents is due to an external factor (the subject's quiddity or an increment of the same kind), which does not apply to quiddities themselves because their essence cannot be increased by something external of a different kind.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, where does the additional amount (al-qadar al-zā'id) that causes intensity in an accident belong?
A) In the concept of the accident itself.
B) In the quiddity of the stronger subject.
C) In the quiddity of the weaker subject.

Key Takeaway
Tashkik in accidents is defended because the additional intensity belongs to the subject's quiddity or is an increment of the same kind, not to the accident's concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the additional intensity in an accident belongs to the subject and not to the accident itself, then your anger, patience, or generosity does not change what those qualities essentially are. When you judge yourself as 'angrier' than others, do you attribute that difference to your own essence or to the circumstances you are subject to? Will you now reconsider how you label yourself and others, knowing that the intensity is not part of the quality's definition?
?
The counter-objection blurred the line between essence and accident, suggesting that external factors could equally differentiate quiddities. In your own life, where do you treat your accidental qualities (role, mood, achievements) as if they were essential to who you are? Are you willing to expose that confusion and ask Allah to show you your true essence?
?
The conclusion warns that denying tashkik in accidents leads to absurdity—no concept could have differing individuals. But do you live as if your own differences from others are absolute, forgetting that you share the same essential human nature? How can this truth about tashkik bring you closer to humility and gratitude?

Gradation in Accidents: The Core Dialectic
Analysis of tashkīk in accidents with the whiteness example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of gradation (tashkīk) applies to accidents and the key objection regarding the additional quantity being external to the shared concept.

Core Idea
Gradation in accidents requires the additional quantity to be external to the shared concept.

This lesson delves into the initial argument about whether accidents can vary in intensity. The author presents an example from whiteness in snow versus ivory and then addresses a critical objection about the nature of the additional quantity. You will see how the response refines our understanding of accidental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the overall flow. Then read the translation to ensure comprehension.
Pay attention to the example of whiteness and the dialectical structure.
2
Identify the claim and objection
The passage begins with a claim that gradation is possible. It then presents an objection: if the extra quality is not in the concept, then all instances are the same. Find these parts.
The objection is implied in the first sentence after 'because'.
3
Understand the answer and extended objection
The answer states that the extra quality enters the essence of the more intense instance. Then an objection extends the same reasoning to essences. Follow the logic.
The extended objection uses the analogy of light and sunlight.
4
Refine the response
The refined answer clarifies that the critical factor is whether the increase is of the same genus as the essence or accident. Note the examples of light, whiteness, and heat.
This leads to the conclusion that such gradation is not impossible in essences either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالتشكيك قابلاً للشدة والضعف لأن القدر الزائد إما داخل في مفهوم العارض فيهما على السواء، مثلا الخصوصية التي توجد في بياض الثلج دون العاج، إن كانت مأخوذة في مفهوم البياض لم يكن ما في العج من معروضاته، وإلا لكان مفهوم البياض فيهما على السواء.
أجيب بأنه داخل في ماهية المعروض الأشد، وإن لم يدخل في ماهية العارض ولا في ماهية المعروض الأضعف، ولا يلزم من عدم دخوله في مفهوم العارض تساويه في جميع المعروضات ولقائل أن يقول: فيتوجه مثله على الدليل المذكور على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذلك لأنه لما جاز التفاوت في العارض باعتبار أمر خارج عنه داخل في ماهية بعض المعروضات فلم لا يجوز في الماهية باعتبار أمر خارج عنها داخل في هوية بعض الأفراد، مثلاً يكون النور تمام ماهية الأنوار أو جنساً لها وتكون الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس أمراً خارجاً عن حقيقة النور، داخلاً في هوية نور الشمس على هذا القياس.
وتوجيه المنع أنا لا نسلم أن القدر الزائد إن كان خارجاً عن الماهية كانت الماهية في الكل على السواء، وإنما يلزم لو لم يكن ذلك زيادة من جنس الماهية، وإذا تحققت فلا عبرة بكونه داخلا في ماهية المعروض حتى لو فرضنا الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس من عوارضه كان التفاوت بحاله، وإنما العبرة بكونه من جنس العارض وزيادة فيه، فإن الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس، وبياض الثلج وحرارة النار ليست إلا زيادة نور وبياض، وحرارة، ولا يمتنع مثل ذلك في الماهية وذاتياتها.
والحاصل: أن عدم دخول القدر الزائد الذي به التفاوت في المعنى المشترك الذي فيه التفاوت، إن كان مانعاً من التفاوت لزم عدم تفاوت شيء من المفهومات في أفراده سواء كان عارضاً لهما، أو ذاتياً، وهو معنى النقض،
وإن لم يكن مانعاً لم يتم الدليل على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذاتياتها، ومن ههنا ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك مطلقاً ممسكاً بالدليل المذكور وجوز بعضهم التشكيك والتفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، نظراً إلى عدم دليل الامتناع، بل ادعوا أن تفاوت الخط الأطول والأقصر تفاوت في الماهية الخطية وأنها في الأطول أكمل وفي الأقصر أنقص، لأن الزيادة التي في الطول من الخط، وإن لم يكن داخلا في ماهيته وإن ادعى التفرقة بين ما إذا كان ذلك القدر الخارج عنه المعنى المشترك داخلا في ماهية الأشد، وبين ما إذا كان داخلاً في مجرد هويته لم يكن بد من البيان مع أن الدليل المذكور لا يتم حينئذ في أجزاء الماهية لجواز أن يكون ما به يتفاوت الجنس خارجاً عنه، داخلاً في ماهية بعض أنواعه.
By tashkik (gradation) it is susceptible to intensity and weakness, because the additional quantity either enters into the concept of the accident in both on par. For example, the particularity which exists in the whiteness of snow but not in [that of] ivory: if it is taken into the concept of whiteness, then what is in ivory would not be among its instantiations; otherwise the concept of whiteness would be the same in both.
It is answered that it enters into the essence of the more intense instantiation, even if it does not enter into the essence of the accident nor into the essence of the weaker instantiation. And it does not follow from its not entering into the concept of the accident that it is equal in all instantiations. And one may say: then the same objection applies to the mentioned proof for the impossibility of variation in essence. That is because when variation in the accident is permitted by virtue of something external to it that enters into the essence of some instantiations, why then is it not permitted in the essence by virtue of something external to it that enters into the quiddity of some individuals? For example, light is the complete essence of lights or a genus for them, and the particularity which is in the light of the sun is something external to the reality of light, entering into the quiddity of the sunlight on this analogy.
And the direction of prevention is that we do not concede that if the additional quantity is external to the essence, then the essence is the same in all. It would only follow if that increase were not of the same genus as the essence. And if this is verified, then no consideration is given to its being part of the essence of the instantiation, such that even if we suppose the particularity in the sunlight to be among its accidents, the variation would remain as it is. Rather, consideration is given to its being of the genus of the accident and an increase in it. For the particularity in the sunlight, the whiteness of snow, and the heat of fire are nothing but an increase of light, whiteness, and heat, and the like is not impossible in the essence and its essential properties.
The upshot: if the non-entering of the additional quantity by which variation occurs into the shared meaning in which variation occurs is a preventer of variation, then it would necessitate the non-variation of any concept among its individuals, whether it is an accident to them or essential, and this is the meaning of the rebuttal. And if it is not a preventer, then the proof for the impossibility of variation of essence and its essential properties is not established. Hence some went to deny tashkik absolutely, holding fast to the mentioned proof, and some allowed tashkik and variation in essence and its essential properties, considering the lack of proof of impossibility. Rather, they claimed that the variation of the longer and shorter line is variation in the linear essence, and that it is more complete in the longer and deficient in the shorter, because the increase in length is of the line, even if it does not enter into its essence. And if one claims a distinction between when that quantity external to the shared meaning enters into the essence of the more intense and when it enters into its mere quiddity, then one must provide proof, especially since the mentioned proof is not then established concerning the parts of the essence, because it is possible that that by which the genus varies is external to it, entering into the essence of some of its species.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the possibility of gradation (tashkik) in essences and accidents. It begins with a claim that variation is possible because an additional quality may or may not be part of the shared concept. An example is given of whiteness in snow vs. ivory. Then an objection is raised: if the extra quality is external to the concept, then the concept is identical in all instances, preventing gradation. The response clarifies that the additional quality enters into the essence of the more intense instantiation, not into the concept of the accident itself. The objector then extends the argument to essences by analogy. The author refines the response: the critical factor is whether the increase is of the same genus as the essence or accident. The upshot summarizes two positions: some deny tashkik absolutely, others allow it in essences, using the example of lines of different lengths. The passage concludes by noting that if a distinction is made between entering into the essence of the more intense and entering its mere quiddity, then proof is required, and the original proof does not hold for parts of the essence.

تشكيك
تفاوت
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an additional quality is not part of the shared concept, then all instances of that accident are identical.
Correction: The additional quality may be part of the essence of the more intense instance, so the instances are not identical even if the concept is the same.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific 'added particularity' in your life could raise the intensity of your iman, even if it means letting go of a comfort you hold dear?
?
The passage argues that variation in accidents requires an increase of the same genus, not a foreign addition. In your own character, have you ever tried to add a virtue that was not truly of the same genus—like seeking status under the guise of humility? How can you strip away the foreign elements and cultivate only a pure increase of what is good?
?
You know that the concept of whiteness is the same in snow and ivory, yet one dazzles and the other does not. Your Islam may share the same core with others, but is it intense or weak? What particularity have you allowed to remain external to your heart, and what decisive change must you make today to internalize that increase?

The Upshot: Two Positions on Gradation in Essences
Summary of the dispute on tashkīk in essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly positions on whether gradation can occur in essences and the line-length example.

Core Idea
Scholars differ on whether gradation applies to essences; some deny it absolutely, others allow it.

This lesson summarizes the debate on gradation in essences. After the refined answer, the author presents the upshot (al-ḥāṣil) and outlines two opposing views. You will also see the intriguing example of longer and shorter lines used to argue for gradation in linear essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the upshot statement
The upshot presents a conditional: if non-inclusion of the added quality blocks variation, then no concept varies; if not, the original proof fails.
This sets the logical framework for the two positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Some scholars deny tashkik absolutely, holding to the original proof. Others allow it in essences, citing examples like longer and shorter lines.
Both positions are presented as viable.
3
Analyze the line example
Those who allow gradation argue that a longer line has a more complete linear essence due to the increase in length, which is of the same genus as the line.
Consider the final remark about the proof not holding for parts of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالتشكيك قابلاً للشدة والضعف لأن القدر الزائد إما داخل في مفهوم العارض فيهما على السواء، مثلا الخصوصية التي توجد في بياض الثلج دون العاج، إن كانت مأخوذة في مفهوم البياض لم يكن ما في العج من معروضاته، وإلا لكان مفهوم البياض فيهما على السواء.
أجيب بأنه داخل في ماهية المعروض الأشد، وإن لم يدخل في ماهية العارض ولا في ماهية المعروض الأضعف، ولا يلزم من عدم دخوله في مفهوم العارض تساويه في جميع المعروضات ولقائل أن يقول: فيتوجه مثله على الدليل المذكور على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذلك لأنه لما جاز التفاوت في العارض باعتبار أمر خارج عنه داخل في ماهية بعض المعروضات فلم لا يجوز في الماهية باعتبار أمر خارج عنها داخل في هوية بعض الأفراد، مثلاً يكون النور تمام ماهية الأنوار أو جنساً لها وتكون الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس أمراً خارجاً عن حقيقة النور، داخلاً في هوية نور الشمس على هذا القياس.
وتوجيه المنع أنا لا نسلم أن القدر الزائد إن كان خارجاً عن الماهية كانت الماهية في الكل على السواء، وإنما يلزم لو لم يكن ذلك زيادة من جنس الماهية، وإذا تحققت فلا عبرة بكونه داخلا في ماهية المعروض حتى لو فرضنا الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس من عوارضه كان التفاوت بحاله، وإنما العبرة بكونه من جنس العارض وزيادة فيه، فإن الخصوصية التي في نور الشمس، وبياض الثلج وحرارة النار ليست إلا زيادة نور وبياض، وحرارة، ولا يمتنع مثل ذلك في الماهية وذاتياتها.
والحاصل: أن عدم دخول القدر الزائد الذي به التفاوت في المعنى المشترك الذي فيه التفاوت، إن كان مانعاً من التفاوت لزم عدم تفاوت شيء من المفهومات في أفراده سواء كان عارضاً لهما، أو ذاتياً، وهو معنى النقض،
وإن لم يكن مانعاً لم يتم الدليل على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذاتياتها، ومن ههنا ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك مطلقاً ممسكاً بالدليل المذكور وجوز بعضهم التشكيك والتفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، نظراً إلى عدم دليل الامتناع، بل ادعوا أن تفاوت الخط الأطول والأقصر تفاوت في الماهية الخطية وأنها في الأطول أكمل وفي الأقصر أنقص، لأن الزيادة التي في الطول من الخط، وإن لم يكن داخلا في ماهيته وإن ادعى التفرقة بين ما إذا كان ذلك القدر الخارج عنه المعنى المشترك داخلا في ماهية الأشد، وبين ما إذا كان داخلاً في مجرد هويته لم يكن بد من البيان مع أن الدليل المذكور لا يتم حينئذ في أجزاء الماهية لجواز أن يكون ما به يتفاوت الجنس خارجاً عنه، داخلاً في ماهية بعض أنواعه.
By tashkik (gradation) it is susceptible to intensity and weakness, because the additional quantity either enters into the concept of the accident in both on par. For example, the particularity which exists in the whiteness of snow but not in [that of] ivory: if it is taken into the concept of whiteness, then what is in ivory would not be among its instantiations; otherwise the concept of whiteness would be the same in both.
It is answered that it enters into the essence of the more intense instantiation, even if it does not enter into the essence of the accident nor into the essence of the weaker instantiation. And it does not follow from its not entering into the concept of the accident that it is equal in all instantiations. And one may say: then the same objection applies to the mentioned proof for the impossibility of variation in essence. That is because when variation in the accident is permitted by virtue of something external to it that enters into the essence of some instantiations, why then is it not permitted in the essence by virtue of something external to it that enters into the quiddity of some individuals? For example, light is the complete essence of lights or a genus for them, and the particularity which is in the light of the sun is something external to the reality of light, entering into the quiddity of the sunlight on this analogy.
And the direction of prevention is that we do not concede that if the additional quantity is external to the essence, then the essence is the same in all. It would only follow if that increase were not of the same genus as the essence. And if this is verified, then no consideration is given to its being part of the essence of the instantiation, such that even if we suppose the particularity in the sunlight to be among its accidents, the variation would remain as it is. Rather, consideration is given to its being of the genus of the accident and an increase in it. For the particularity in the sunlight, the whiteness of snow, and the heat of fire are nothing but an increase of light, whiteness, and heat, and the like is not impossible in the essence and its essential properties.
The upshot: if the non-entering of the additional quantity by which variation occurs into the shared meaning in which variation occurs is a preventer of variation, then it would necessitate the non-variation of any concept among its individuals, whether it is an accident to them or essential, and this is the meaning of the rebuttal. And if it is not a preventer, then the proof for the impossibility of variation of essence and its essential properties is not established. Hence some went to deny tashkik absolutely, holding fast to the mentioned proof, and some allowed tashkik and variation in essence and its essential properties, considering the lack of proof of impossibility. Rather, they claimed that the variation of the longer and shorter line is variation in the linear essence, and that it is more complete in the longer and deficient in the shorter, because the increase in length is of the line, even if it does not enter into its essence. And if one claims a distinction between when that quantity external to the shared meaning enters into the essence of the more intense and when it enters into its mere quiddity, then one must provide proof, especially since the mentioned proof is not then established concerning the parts of the essence, because it is possible that that by which the genus varies is external to it, entering into the essence of some of its species.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the possibility of gradation (tashkik) in essences and accidents. It begins with a claim that variation is possible because an additional quality may or may not be part of the shared concept. An example is given of whiteness in snow vs. ivory. Then an objection is raised: if the extra quality is external to the concept, then the concept is identical in all instances, preventing gradation. The response clarifies that the additional quality enters into the essence of the more intense instantiation, not into the concept of the accident itself. The objector then extends the argument to essences by analogy. The author refines the response: the critical factor is whether the increase is of the same genus as the essence or accident. The upshot summarizes two positions: some deny tashkik absolutely, others allow it in essences, using the example of lines of different lengths. The passage concludes by noting that if a distinction is made between entering into the essence of the more intense and entering its mere quiddity, then proof is required, and the original proof does not hold for parts of the essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The upshot proves that gradation in essences is impossible.
Correction: The upshot only summarizes the logical implications; both positions are presented as viable, and the example of lines shows a plausible case for gradation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do those who allow gradation in essences use as an example?
A) Whiteness in snow and ivory
B) Heat in fire and water
C) Longer and shorter lines
D) Light of the sun and moon

Key Takeaway
Whether gradation applies to essences remains disputed; the upshot shows the logical consequences of each assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The upshot shows that a seemingly solid proof can be challenged by a deeper conditional. When you hold a theological conviction, do you ever examine the hidden assumptions behind your reasoning? Will you commit to seeking out opposing viewpoints to test your certainty?
?
Some scholars in this lesson cling to the original proof, while others allow variation due to lack of evidence. In your own life, are you more inclined to hold rigidly to a position or to remain open when proofs are inconclusive? Which approach brings you closer to truth, and which one might be driven by pride?
?
The example of the longer and shorter line suggests that something external can still belong to the essence. Reflect: Are there aspects of your faith—like your deeds or character—that you consider 'external' to your identity, but which actually define who you truly are? What would it mean to see them as essential?

The Nature of Doubt (Shakk) in Islamic Thought
A definition of shakk (doubt) from al-Jurjani and Descartes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of doubt (shakk) as hesitation between two opposites without rational preference, and how it relates to ignorance, conjecture, and certainty.

Core Idea
Doubt is a specific form of ignorance where the mind is equally balanced between two opposing claims.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents the definition of shakk (doubt) as used in logic and kalam. It begins by defining doubt as hesitation between two opposites due to equal evidence or lack thereof, then distinguishes it from related terms like ignorance and conjecture, and finally references Descartes' view of doubt as an act of the will. Understanding this concept is foundational for grasping epistemological categories in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Focus on the definition and the examples given.
Notice the two causes of doubt: equal indications or no indications.
2
Identify the two causes of doubt
The passage states that doubt arises either from equal indications ('amarat mutasāwiya) for both sides, or from a complete absence of any indication. Reflect on how these differ.
In the first case, evidence is present but balanced; in the second, no evidence exists.
3
Understand the hierarchy of states
The passage contrasts doubt with ignorance (jahl), conjecture (ẓann), and predominant conjecture (ghālib al-ẓann). Note the logical relationships: every doubt is ignorance but not vice versa; doubt is balanced, conjecture leans but does not reject the other, predominant conjecture leans and rejects the other side.
This hierarchy is crucial for assessing the strength of beliefs.
4
Consider Descartes' perspective
The passage adds Descartes' view that doubt is an act of the will applied to judgments, not to mere ideas. Reflect on how this differs from the classical logical definition.
Descartes' doubt is methodological and voluntary, while the classical definition is epistemic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشك: هو التردد بين نقيضين لا يرجح العقل أحدهما على الآخر، وذلك لوجود أمارات متساوية في الحكمين، أو لعدم وجود أية أمارة فيهما، ويرجع تردد العقل بين الحكمين إلى عجزه عن معاناة التحليل أو إلى قناعته بالجهل. لذلك قيل: الشك ضرب من الجهل، إلا أنه أخص منه، لأن كل شك جهل ولا عكس. وقيل: الشك ما استوى طرفاه، وهو الوقوف بين الشيئين لا يميل القلب إلى أحدهما فإذا ترجح أحدهما ولم يطرح الآخر فهو ظن، فإذا طرحه فهو غالب الظن، وهو بمنزلة اليقين (تعريفات الجرجاني). والشك عند ديكارت فعل من أفعال الإدارة، فهو ينصب على الأحكام لا على التصورات والأفكار لأن التصورات من غير حكم لا تسمى صادقة ولا كاذبة. راجع ديكارت عثمان أمين ص (۱۰۲).
Doubt is hesitation between two opposites which the intellect does not prefer one over the other, either due to the existence of equal indications in both judgments, or due to the absence of any indication in them. The intellect's hesitation between the two judgments is due to its incapacity to undertake analysis or to its satisfaction with ignorance. Therefore it is said: doubt is a kind of ignorance, except that it is more specific than it, because every doubt is ignorance, but not vice versa. And it is said: doubt is that whose two sides are equal, and it is standing between two things without the heart inclining to either. If one of them preponderates without rejecting the other, it is conjecture. If it [the other] is rejected, it is predominating conjecture, and it is in the position of certainty. (al-Jurjānī's Definitions). And doubt according to Descartes is an act of the will; it concerns judgments, not mental representations and ideas, because representations without judgment are neither true nor false. Refer to Descartes by Uthman Amin, p. 102.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'shakk' (doubt) as used in logic and kalam. It states that doubt is hesitation between two opposing propositions without rational preference, due to equal evidence or lack of evidence. It distinguishes doubt from ignorance (every doubt is ignorance but not vice versa) and from conjecture (ẓann) and predominating conjecture (ghālib al-ẓann). The definition from al-Jurjānī is cited, and a modern reference to Descartes is added, describing doubt as an act of the will concerning judgments, not mere ideas.

Key Terms

تردد
ظن
يقين
أمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think doubt is simply lacking knowledge or being ignorant.
Correction: Doubt is specifically a balanced hesitation between two options, not mere ignorance. Ignorance is a broader category that includes doubt but also includes states like total unawareness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a state considered doubt?
A) When the intellect prefers one side.
B) When there are equal indications or no indications.
C) When the heart inclines to one side.
D) When a judgment is certain.

Key Takeaway
Doubt is a state of equal hesitation between two opposites, distinct from ignorance, conjecture, and certainty, and is caused by equal or absent evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious doubt that feels like a true shakk—where evidence seems equal on both sides—do you tend to investigate further (analysis) or do you settle for ignorance? Which response does this lesson call you to?
?
The passage says the intellect's hesitation is due to its 'incapacity to undertake analysis' or its 'satisfaction with ignorance.' Which of these two describes your own approach when you don't have clear answers in matters of faith? What will you do about it starting today?
?
If doubt is a specific form of ignorance, then seeking certainty becomes an intellectual and spiritual duty. Are you actively seeking to replace your doubts with yaqin, or have you made peace with shakk in areas where you could find answers? Commit to one step you will take this week.

Two Views on Gradation in Essence
Presents two positions on tashkik in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions on whether essence (mahiyya) can admit gradation (tashkik) and the logical basis for each.

Core Idea
The debate on tashkik hinges on whether the proof against variation is conclusive or not.

In this passage, the author presents the core disagreement about tashkik in essence. Some scholars insist that essence cannot vary in intensity, relying on a proof that variation leads to contradiction. Others argue that there is no such proof, so variation remains possible. This foundational debate sets the stage for the famous line example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two groups
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two contrasting views: one group denies tashkik absolutely, the other permits it. Mark the phrases that indicate each position.
The phrase 'ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك مطلقاً' indicates the first group.
2
Understand the logical structure
The passage begins with a conditional: if there is no preventing factor, the proof against variation fails. Then it introduces the two groups. Connect this conditional to the groups' reasoning.
The proof mentioned is likely the argument that variation would entail composition or contradiction.
3
Ask: why does the second group permit tashkik?
The second group permits tashkik simply because there is no proof of impossibility. This shows that in kalam, the burden of proof matters: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
This is a key epistemological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكن مانعاً لم يتم الدليل على امتناع تفاوت الماهية وذاتياتها، ومن ههنا ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك مطلقاً ممسكاً بالدليل المذكور وجوز بعضهم التشكيك والتفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، نظراً إلى عدم دليل الامتناع، بل ادعوا أن تفاوت الخط الأطول والأقصر تفاوت في الماهية الخطية وأنها في الأطول أكمل وفي الأقصر أنقص، لأن الزيادة التي في الطول من الخط، وإن لم يكن داخلاً في ماهيته وإن ادعى التفرقة بين ما إذا كان ذلك القدر الخارج عنه المعنى المشترك داخلاً في ماهية الأشد، وبين ما إذا كان داخلاً في مجرد هويته لم يكن بد من البيان مع أن الدليل المذكور لا يتم حينئذ في أجزاء الماهية لجواز أن يكون ما به يتفاوت الجنس خارجاً عنه، داخلاً في ماهية بعض أنواعه.
And if there is no preventing factor, the proof for the impossibility of variation in the essence and its essential properties is not complete. From here, some went to deny tashkik (gradation) absolutely, adhering to the mentioned proof, and some permitted tashkik and variation in the essence and its essential properties, due to the absence of a proof of impossibility. Rather, they claimed that the variation between the longer and shorter line is variation in the essential nature of line, and that it is more perfect in the longer and more deficient in the shorter, because the increase which is in the length of the line, even if it is not included in its essence, and if one claims a distinction between whether that quantity external to the common meaning is included in the essence of the more intense, or whether it is included merely in its identity, there must be a clarification, while the mentioned proof is not complete then with respect to the parts of the essence, because it is possible that what differentiates the genus is external to it, being included in the essence of some of its species.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage examines the debate over whether essential natures (mahiyyat) can admit gradation (tashkik). The author first states a conditional: if no obstacle exists, the proof against variation is inconclusive. Then he presents two opposing views. One group denies tashkik absolutely, relying on a proof (presumably the one that variation would entail composition or contradiction). Another group permits tashkik, arguing that there is no clear proof of impossibility. They offer the example of a line: a longer line and a shorter line both share the essence of 'line', yet they differ in perfection due to length. The objection raises the issue of whether the extra length is part of the essence. If the length is external to the common meaning (the genus), and if one tries to distinguish between its being part of the essence of the more intense (the longer line as a species) versus merely its haecceity (huwiyya), the proof fails because the differentiating factor may be outside the genus but internal to the species. The passage ends by suggesting that the genus-differentiating element can be external to the genus yet enter the essence of some species, thus allowing for gradation.

Key Terms
ماهية
ذاتيات
تشكيك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Variation in essence always implies a change in the core definition, making it impossible.
Correction: The second group argues that variation can occur without changing the essence, as shown by the line example where longer and shorter lines both remain 'line'.

Checkpoint
What reason do those who permit tashkik give for their position?
A) They have a proof that variation is necessary.
B) There is no proof of impossibility.
C) The line example proves variation is impossible.
D) They deny the existence of essence.

Key Takeaway
The debate over tashkik in essence centers on whether the proof against variation is conclusive; without it, variation remains a logical possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a flaw in your character, do you treat it as an accidental part of your identity that can be changed, or as something fixed in your essence? This lesson forces you to ask: does your belief about your own nature shape the effort you invest in change?
?
The example of the longer and shorter line shows that two things can share an essence yet differ in perfection. What does that imply about your own journey? If your essence is not static, what are you doing to lengthen your line toward perfection—or are you content with being shorter?
?
The passage says the proof against variation is not conclusive. Think about a belief you hold about yourself that you consider 'true' even though you have no conclusive proof. Is it possible you are limiting yourself by denying gradation in your own essence? What would change if you treated your essence as capable of growth?

The Line Argument and Its Critique
Analyses the line example and its implications for tashkik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the longer and shorter line is used to argue for tashkik in essence, and the subtle objection about whether the extra quantity belongs to the essence or the identity.

Core Idea
The line example shows that two things can share an essence yet differ in perfection, challenging the denial of tashkik.

The second group in the tashkik debate offers a concrete example: a longer line and a shorter line both share the essence 'line', but differ in perfection due to length. This raises a key question: does the extra length become part of the essence of the longer line, or is it merely an accidental property? The passage explores this nuance and concludes that the proof against tashkik is incomplete because the differentiating factor may belong to the species essence.

Guided Path
1
Examine the line example
Read the part of the passage that mentions the longer and shorter line. Notice how it claims that the variation in length constitutes variation in the essential nature of 'line' itself.
The claim: 'تفاوت الخط الأطول والأقصر تفاوت في الماهية الخطية'.
2
Understand the distinction: essence vs. identity
The passage introduces a distinction: is the extra length part of the essence (mahiyya) of the longer line, or merely part of its identity (huwiyya)? This is a subtle but crucial point.
The terms: 'ماهية الأشد' vs. 'مجرد هويته'.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
The author argues that if one claims the extra length is external to the common meaning (the genus), then the proof against tashkik fails for parts of essence. Because what differentiates the genus (the differentia) can be outside the genus but inside the species essence.
This allows for gradation in essence across species of the same genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if there is no preventing factor, the proof for the impossibility of variation in the essence and its essential properties is not complete. From here, some went to deny tashkik (gradation) absolutely, adhering to the mentioned proof, and some permitted tashkik and variation in the essence and its essential properties, due to the absence of a proof of impossibility. This passage examines the debate over whether essential natures (mahiyyat) can admit gradation (tashkik). Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share an essence, they must be identical in all aspects, so any variation implies a different essence.
Correction: The line example shows that variation in perfection (like length) can occur within the same essence, provided the differentiator is not part of the common meaning but of the species.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the example of the longer and shorter line illustrate?
A) That line is a genus with two species.
B) That variation in length is variation in the essence of line.
C) That length is not an essential property of line.
D) That the longer line has a different essence than the shorter line.

Key Takeaway
The line example challenges the denial of tashkik by showing that variation in perfection can coexist with a shared essence, supported by the possibility that the differentiator belongs to the species essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own understanding of Islam as if it is either perfect or worthless, ignoring the possibility of gradation in knowledge and practice? How could embracing the concept of tashkik change your approach to learning and growing in religion?
?
The longer line is more perfect in its essence—do you see your own faith as something that can be more or less perfect without losing its essential nature? What would it mean for you to strive for greater perfection in your faith without feeling that you have lost the essence?
?
In what areas of your life do you deny the possibility of gradual improvement, expecting either perfection or nothing? How might accepting gradation in essence free you from despair or pride in your spiritual journey?
