Refuting Al-Farabi: Utterance and Quantity
The author refutes Al-Farabi's claim that utterance belongs to the category of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that utterance is not intrinsically a quantity, even though it can be measured by syllables.

Core Idea
Being measurable does not make something essentially a quantity; only essential measurement qualifies.

Al-Farabi argued that since utterances can be measured by syllables, they belong to the category of quantity. But the author rebuts this by distinguishing between essential and accidental measurement. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its significance in theological logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'كم', 'تقدير', and 'بالعرض'.
2
Understand Al-Farabi's argument
Identify Al-Farabi's claim that utterance is quantity because it can be measured by parts (syllables). Note his reasoning about short and long syllables composing larger units.
He thinks measurability by a part is sufficient for being a quantity.
3
Identify the rebuttal
The author denies the major premise: for something to be a quantity, the measurement must be essential, not accidental. Here, the measurement of utterance by syllables is merely accidental.
The author uses the analogy of a body measured by a cubit – the body's continuous quantity makes it measurable, but the measurement is accidental to its essence.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: what does it mean for measurement to be essential vs accidental? This distinction is crucial in kalam to avoid misclassifying things.
Utterance has multiplicity of parts, but that multiplicity is not what makes it a quantity essentially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ من قبيل الكم قال: وزعم الفارابي أن اللفظ من قبيل الكم وهو ما يمكن أن يقدر جميعه (۲) بجزء منه إذ كل لفظ مقدر بمقطع مقصور أو ممدود أو بما تركب منهما، ورد بأنه بالعرض كالجسم). إن القول من مقولة الكم، وإن الكم المنفصل أيضاً ينقسم إلى قار هـو العدد وغير قار هو القول واحتج بأن (ذو) جزء (۳) يتقدر بجزئه وكل ما هو كذلك فهو كم وفاقاً . بيان الصغرى أن أجزاء الأقاويل مقاطع مقصورة أو ممدودة يقع ) فيها التركيب بأن يردف من (٥) مقصور بممدود مثل على أو بالعكس مثل كان ثم تركب هذه المقاطع مرة (٦) أخرى فيحدث أشياء أعظم مما تقدم فأصغر ما يتقدر به الألفاظ هي المقاطع البسيطة المقصورة ثم الممدودة. ثم بعدها المركبة، وأكملها ما ذكر فيه المقصور أو لا ثم أردف بالممدود، والقول ربما يتقدر بواحد منها، وربما يحتاج إلى أن يقدر باثنين أو أكثر كسائر المقادير، فإن منها ما يقدره ذراع فيستغرقه (۷) ، ومنها ما يحتاج إلى ذراعين وأكثر. وأجيب بمنع الكبرى، وإنما ذلك إذا كان التقدير (۸) لذاته، وهاهنا إنما عرضي للعقول خاصة الكم من جهة الكثرة التي فيه (۹) ، كما أن الجسم يتقدر بالذراع ونحوه لما فيه من الكم المتصل.
Utterance is of the category of quantity. He said: Al-Farabi claimed that utterance is of the category of quantity, which is that whose entirety can be measured by a part of it, since every utterance is measured by a short or long syllable or by what is composed of them. It was rebutted as being accidental, like the body. Indeed, speech is of the category of quantity, and discrete quantity is also divided into constant (which is number) and non-constant (which is speech). He argued that 'dhū' is a part that is measured by its part, and everything that is so is a quantity by agreement. The minor premise: the parts of speeches are short or long syllables, in which composition occurs, by following a short with a long like 'alā' or the reverse like 'kān'. Then these syllables are composed again, producing things greater than what preceded. Thus the smallest by which utterances are measured are the simple short syllables, then the long ones, then the compound ones. The most complete is that in which the short is mentioned first, then followed by the long. Speech may be measured by one of them, or may need to be measured by two or more, like other measures: some are measured by a cubit and exhaust it, some need two cubits or more. It was answered by denying the major premise: that would be if the measurement were essential, but here it is only accidental to the intellects specifically, from the aspect of the multiplicity in it, just as the body is measured by the cubit and the like due to what is in it of continuous quantity.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Farabi's argument that utterance (اللفظ) belongs to the category of quantity (الكم) and its refutation. Al-Farabi claims that every utterance is measurable by a syllable, so it is a quantity. The refutation states that this is accidental, not essential. The author elaborates Al-Farabi's reasoning: speech (القول) is a discrete non-constant quantity, and its parts are syllables that compose larger units, measurable by syllables as units. But the rebuttal denies the major premise: measurement would be essential only for quantity per se; here it is accidental, due to the multiplicity of syllables, just as a body is measured by a cubit due to its continuous quantity, not essentially. Thus, utterance is not intrinsically a quantity.

Key Terms
كم
قول
مقطع
تقدير
بالعرض
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something can be measured by a part, it necessarily belongs to the category of quantity.
Correction: The author distinguishes between essential and accidental measurement. For utterance, measurement by syllables is accidental, not essential to its nature.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject Al-Farabi's view that utterance is a quantity?
A) Because utterance cannot be measured
B) Because measurement is accidental, not essential
C) Because syllables are not parts of utterance
D) Because quantity is only continuous

Key Takeaway
Utterance is not essentially a quantity; its measurability by syllables is accidental, not intrinsic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often measure your speech by how many words you say or how long you talk. But this lesson teaches that such measurement is accidental to the essence of speech. In your daily conversations, do you prioritize the quantity of your words or their meaning? Will you commit to speaking with intention, not just filling silence?
?
Al-Farabi's argument seems logical at first glance, yet it is refuted because it confuses accidental with essential. In your own thinking, where might you be mistaking an accidental property for an essential one—especially regarding your own actions or beliefs? How can you guard against this error?
?
The rebuttal states that speech is measured by syllables only due to multiplicity, not because speech itself is quantity. How often do you measure your worship (like prayers) by quantity (number of rak'ahs) without considering its essence (khushu')? What will you do to ensure your worship is not just measured accidentally?

Al-Farabi's Claim and Its Rebuttal: Is Speech a Quantity?
Al-Taftazani reports al-Farabi's claim that speech is quantity and gives the rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Farabi argued that speech belongs to the category of quantity and how the rebuttal refutes this by distinguishing essential from accidental measurement.

Core Idea
Speech is only accidentally a quantity, not essentially.

Al-Farabi, the renowned philosopher, proposed that speech (al-lafẓ) falls under the category of quantity, arguing that it can be measured by syllables. This lesson presents his argument and the rebuttal that such measurement is accidental, akin to a body being measured by a cubit. This distinction between essential and accidental attributes is crucial in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the flow from claim to supporting argument to rebuttal.
The passage is dense with logical terminology; take your time.
2
Identify al-Farabi's claim and evidence
Locate where al-Farabi states that speech is quantity and note the reasoning involving syllables and measurement.
He argues that a word can be measured by its smallest part, the syllable.
3
Understand the rebuttal's reasoning
Examine how the rebuttal denies the major premise, asserting that measurement applies only when essential, not accidental.
The analogy of a body measured by a cubit helps illustrate accidental measurement.
4
Reflect on essential vs. accidental
Consider the broader theological significance: attributing quantity to speech accidentally preserves its non-material nature.
This distinction prevents categorizing speech as a purely physical entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللفظ من قبيل الكم
قال: وزعم الفارابي أن اللفظ من قبيل الكم وهو ما يمكن أن يقدر جميعه بجزء منه إذ كل لفظ مقدر بمقطع مقصور أو ممدود أو بما تركب منهما، ورد بأنه بالعرض كالجسم.
إن القول من مقولة الكم، وإن الكم المنفصل أيضاً ينقسم إلى قار هو العدد وغير قار هو القول واحتج بأن (ذو) جزء يتقدر بجزئه وكل ما هو كذلك فهو كم وفاقاً .
بيان الصغرى أن أجزاء الأقاويل مقاطع مقصورة أو ممدودة يقع فيها التركيب بأن يردف من مقصور بممدود مثل على أو بالعكس مثل كان ثم تركب هذه المقاطع مرة أخرى فيحدث أشياء أعظم مما تقدم فأصغر ما يتقدر به الألفاظ هي المقاطع البسيطة المقصورة ثم الممدودة. ثم بعدها المركبة، وأكملها ما ذكر فيه المقصور أو لا ثم أردف بالممدود، والقول ربما يتقدر بواحد منها، وربما يحتاج إلى أن يقدر باثنين أو أكثر كسائر المقادير، فإن منها ما يقدره ذراع فيستغرقه، ومنها ما يحتاج إلى ذراعين وأكثر.
وأجيب بمنع الكبرى، وإنما ذلك إذا كان التقدير لذاته، وهاهنا إنما عرضي للعقول خاصة الكم من جهة الكثرة التي فيه، كما أن الجسم يتقدر بالذراع ونحوه لما فيه من الكم المتصل.
The word (al-lafẓ) belongs to the category of quantity. He said: Al-Fārābī claimed that the word belongs to the category of quantity, namely that which can be measured in its entirety by a part of it, since every word is measured by a short syllable (maqṭūʿ maqṣūr) or a long syllable (maqṭūʿ mamdūd) or what is composed of them. It was rebutted that it is accidental, like a body. Indeed, speech (al-qawl) is from the category of quantity, and that discrete quantity is also divided into stable, which is number, and non-stable, which is speech. He argued that since it has a part, it is measured by its part, and everything that is so is a quantity by agreement. Explanation of the minor premise: The parts of discourses are short or long syllables; composition occurs in them such that a short syllable is followed by a long syllable, like 'alā', or the opposite, like 'kāna'. Then these syllables are combined again, producing larger things than before. So the smallest by which words are measured are the simple short syllables, then the long ones. Then after them the composite ones. The most complete is that in which the short is mentioned first and then followed by the long. Speech may be measured by one of them, or may need to be measured by two or more, like other measures, for some are measured by a cubit and cover it, and some need two cubits or more. It was answered by denying the major premise. Rather, that measurement applies if it is essential (li-dhātihi). Here it is only accidental, pertaining especially to quantity with respect to the multiplicity in it, just as a body is measured by a cubit and the like due to what is in it of continuous quantity.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses al-Farabi's claim that speech belongs to the category of quantity. It begins by stating al-Farabi's view: the word is a quantity because it can be measured in its entirety by a part of it (e.g., a syllable). Then it notes that this was rebutted as accidental, like a body. The text then explains the supporting argument: that speech is from the category of quantity, and discrete quantity is divided into stable (number) and non-stable (speech). The argument proceeds by stating the minor premise: parts of discourses are syllables, which are short or long, and composition occurs, leading to larger structures. The smallest measures are syllables, and speech may be measured by one or more of them. The response is to deny the major premise: measurement applies only when it is essential, but here it is accidental—speech is quantity only accidentally due to the multiplicity in it, just as a body is measured by a cubit because of the continuous quantity in it. Thus, the claim that speech is essentially quantity is refuted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech is essentially a quantity because it can be measured by syllables.
Correction: Measurement of speech is accidental, not essential; it is quantity only due to the multiplicity in it, just as a body is measured accidentally via its continuous quantity.

Checkpoint
What is the main rebuttal to al-Farabi's claim that speech is quantity?
A) It is refuted by denying the minor premise.
B) It is argued that measurement applies only when essential, and here it is accidental.
C) It is argued that measurement applies only when essential, not accidental.
D) It is said that syllables are not parts of speech.

Key Takeaway
Al-Farabi's claim that speech is quantity is refuted because measurement applies only essentially, and speech is only accidentally a quantity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Farabi reduced speech to measurable syllables, but the rebuttal shows its quantity is accidental. Your words are not just sounds; each will be accounted for. How does this truth change the way you speak today—will you weigh your words before uttering them?
?
If speech is only accidentally quantity, then its essence is meaning. In your prayers and dhikr, do you focus on the quantity of utterances or the quality of presence? What one step can you take to shift from counting words to connecting with their significance?
?
The body is measured by a cubit due to its continuous quantity—an external measure. Your speech may be measured in syllables, but its true measure is its impact. Will you let the world measure your words by their quantity, or will you let Allah measure them by their sincerity and intention?

The Fifth Type: Smells and the Nuance of Sensory Terms
Defines smells and explains how sensory terms apply to quality, locus, or both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how smells are classified and how sensory verbs can refer to the quality, the locus, or both.

Core Idea
Sensory terms can point to the quality itself, its location, or both.

Having discussed four types of qualities, Sanusi turns to smells—a type that lacks named varieties. But more importantly, he uses this opportunity to clarify a subtle point about all five sensory terms: do they refer to the quality, the thing perceived, or both? The answer varies, and getting it right prevents confusion in philosophical discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from smells to a general remark about all sensory terms.
2
Understand the classification of smells
Identify how smells are described: by pleasantness/unpleasantness, by similarity, or by attribution. Note that they lack specific names.
This contrasts with other qualities that have named types.
3
Focus on the general rule about sensory terms
The passage explains that although sensory terms are used as names for the five types of qualities, they actually differ in reference: some refer to quality, some to locus, some to both.
This is a key philosophical nuance.
4
Study the examples
Go through each of the five examples given (sight, hearing, touch, taste, smell) and see how they illustrate the different applications.
This is the most concrete part of the lesson.
5
Reflect on why this nuance matters
Consider how misinterpreting a sensory term could lead to confusion in theological or philosophical arguments about perception and qualities.
This connects to broader kalam concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس المشمومات وهي الروائح، ولا أسماء لأنواعها إلا من جهة الملاءمة، والمنافرة كرائحة طيبة ومنتنة أو المجاورة كرائحة حلوة، أو الإضافة كرائحة المسك. وليس فيها محل بحث، واعلم أنهم وإن أجروا هذه الأوصاف أعني المبصرات والمسموعات والملموسات والمذوقات والمشموات على الأنواع الخمسة من الكيفيات بل جعلوها بمنزلة الأسماء لها فهي بحسب متفاوتة في الوقوع على الكيفية أو على المحل، أو عليهما جميعاً، وفي كون مصادرها موضوعة لذلك النوع من الإدراك كالإبصار والسماع، أو لما يفضي إليه كالبواقي ومن ها هنا يقال : أبصرت الورد وحمرته وسمعت الصوت لا مصوته ولمست الحرير لا لينه وذقت الطعام وحلاوته وشممت العبير ورائحته.
The fifth type: smells, which are odours. They have no names for their varieties except in terms of pleasantness and unpleasantness, like a pleasant or foul odour, or by similarity like a sweet odour, or by attribution like the odour of musk. There is no scope for investigation in this. Know that although they apply these descriptions — namely visual, auditory, tactile, gustatory, and olfactory — to the five types of qualities, indeed they treat them as names for them, they are, however, varying in terms of whether they refer to the quality itself, or to the locus, or to both, and in terms of whether their sources are coined for that type of perception, like sight and hearing, or for what leads to it, like the rest. Hence it is said: I saw the rose and its redness, I heard the sound not the resounder, I touched the silk not its softness, I tasted the food and its sweetness, and I smelled the perfume and its odour.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage first defines the fifth type of quality (al-mashmūmāt), which are odours. Unlike the previous types, odours lack specific names for their varieties; they are only described by their pleasantness or unpleasantness (like pleasant or foul), by analogy (like sweet), or by attribution to a source (like the odour of musk). The author notes that there is no need for deeper investigation into this type. Then the text transitions to a general remark about the five sensory terms (al-mubsirāt, al-masmū'āt, etc.). It explains that although these terms are used as names for the five types of qualities, they actually differ in what they denote. Some terms refer directly to the quality itself (e.g., 'redness' from 'seeing'), some refer to the locus (e.g., 'touch' applies to the silk, not its softness), and some refer to both (e.g., 'saw the rose and its redness'). The author also notes that the sources (maṣādir) of these terms are either coined specifically for that type of perception (like 'sight' for seeing) or for what leads to that perception (like 'touch' leading to tactile quality). He illustrates with examples: 'I saw the rose and its redness' (applies to both), 'I heard the sound not the resounder' (applies to quality, not locus), 'I touched the silk not its softness' (applies to locus, not quality), 'I tasted the food and its sweetness' (applies to both), 'I smelled the perfume and its odour' (applies to both). The passage thus clarifies a linguistic and philosophical nuance regarding the application of perceptual verbs.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sensory terms like 'auditory' always refer to the quality, but the author shows that usage varies between quality, locus, or both.
Correction: The application of sensory terms is nuanced; they can refer to the quality itself, the locus, or both, depending on the specific verb and context.

Checkpoint

A) I heard the sound not the resounder
B) I touched the silk not its softness
C) I tasted the food and its sweetness
D) I smelled the perfume and its odour

Key Takeaway
Smells lack specific names, but more importantly, sensory terms can refer to the quality, the locus, or both, depending on the verb used.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the beauty of a sunset, do you merely 'see' the colors (the locus) or do you truly perceive the grandeur of Allah's creation (the quality)? How can you train your heart to appreciate both, and where does gratitude fit in?
?
The author notes that smells lack specific names. In your spiritual life, do you reduce the 'fragrance' of the Prophet's teachings to mere pleasant feelings, or do you strive to name and internalize their deeper qualities?
?
Consider your use of language in worship. When you say 'I tasted the sweetness of prayer,' are you referring to the physical act or the spiritual quality? How can you ensure your perception remains holistic, not reducing faith to a single dimension?

Defining the Fifth Type: Smells (al-Mashmumat)
Definition of smells and their naming conventions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the category of smells is defined as a quality type and how its subtypes are named.

Core Idea
Smells are a quality type named only by pleasantness, adjacency, or attribution.

The author continues his classification of qualities by introducing the fifth type: smells (al-mashmumat). Unlike other sensory qualities, smells have no fixed names for their subtypes; instead, they are described by their effect (pleasant/unpleasant), by association (e.g., sweet), or by attribution (e.g., musk). This passage establishes the unique linguistic nature of odors.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
The passage defines smells and explains their naming conventions. Notice the examples given.
2
Identify the three ways smells are named
Find the three categories: by pleasantness/unpleasantness, by adjacency, by attribution.
The text explicitly lists them.
3
Understand why there is no investigation
The author says there is no topic of research in smells themselves, likely because they are not independently classifiable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس : المشمومات وهي الروائح، ولا أسماء لأنواعها إلا من جهة الملاءمة، والمنافرة كرائحة طيبة ومنتنة أو المجاورة كرائحة حلوة، أو الإضافة كرائحة المسك . وليس فيها محل بحث، واعلم أنهم وإن أجروا هذه الأوصاف أعني المبصرات والمسموعات والملموسات والمذوقات والمشموات على الأنواع الخمسة من الكيفيات بل جعلوها بمنزلة الأسماء لها فهي بحسب متفاوتة في الوقوع على الكيفية أو على المحل، أو عليهما جميعاً، وفي كون مصادرها موضوعة لذلك النوع من الإدراك كالإبصار والسماع، أو لما يفضي إليه كالبواقي ومن ها هنا يقال : أبصرت الورد وحمرته وسمعت الصوت لا مصوته ولمست الحرير لا لينه وذقت الطعام وحلاوته وشممت العبير ورائحته .
The fifth type: those that are smelled, and they are odors. They have no names for their types except in terms of pleasantness and unpleasantness, like a pleasant smell and a foul smell, or by adjacency like a sweet smell, or by attribution like the smell of musk. And there is no topic of investigation in them. Know that although they have applied these descriptions, namely the visible, audible, tangible, tasteable, and smellable, to the five types of qualities, rather they made them like names for them, yet they are different in terms of applying to the quality or to the locus or to both, and in whether their sources (masadir) are coined for that type of perception like sight and hearing, or for what leads to it like the rest. And from here it is said: I saw the rose and its redness, and I heard the sound not its source, and I touched the silk not its softness, and I tasted the food and its sweetness, and I smelled the perfume and its odor.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of qualities, namely smells (al-mashmumat). The author first states that smells have no names for their subspecies except based on pleasantness/unpleasantness, adjacency (e.g., sweet smell), or attribution (e.g., musk). He then notes that there is no separate topic of investigation (mabahith) for smells. Next, he discusses a broader linguistic-philosophical point: although the terms for the five sensory qualities (visible, audible, etc.) are used as names for those types of qualities, their actual reference varies. Some terms apply to the quality itself (e.g., sound), some to the locus (object) containing the quality, and some to both. Additionally, the verbal nouns (masadir) for some senses (like 'sight' and 'hearing') are coined directly for that type of perception, while for others (taste, touch, smell) they are coined for what leads to the perception (e.g., 'tasting' for food). The author illustrates with examples: 'I saw the rose and its redness' (object and quality), 'I heard the sound not its source' (quality only), 'I touched the silk not its softness' (object only), 'I tasted the food and its sweetness' (both object and quality), 'I smelled the perfume and its odor' (both). This clarifies the nuanced usage of sensory terms in Arabic.

Correction: The passage explains that smells are only named by pleasantness, adjacency, or attribution, not by unique terms.

Smells are a quality type named not by specific terms but by their effect, resemblance, or source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How Sensory Terms Refer to Qualities and Objects
Linguistic classification of sensory terms by reference and derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms for sensory qualities (visible, audible, etc.) can refer to the quality itself, the locus, or both, and how their verbal nouns are coined.

Core Idea
Sensory terms vary in what they refer to — quality, object, or both.

Having introduced the five types of qualities, the author now addresses a subtle linguistic point about how we use sensory terms. The same word for a sensory quality may refer to the quality itself, the object that possesses it, or both. Additionally, the sources (masadir) for these terms are either directly tied to the perception or to what leads to it. This lesson unpacks these distinctions through clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Focus on the author's statement that the sensory descriptions are applied to the five types of qualities but they vary in reference.
2
Understand the variation in reference
Identify the three possibilities: applies to quality alone, to locus alone, to both.
3
Understand the two types of masadir
Note the difference between masadir coined for the perception (like ibsar) and those coined for what leads to it (like dhawq).
4
Examine the examples
For each sense, the author gives an example showing the reference. Match each example to its pattern.

Source Text

Psychical Qualities: Disposition vs. State
Defines psychical qualities and distinguishes between malaka and haal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what psychical qualities are, how they differ from other qualities, and the distinction between a firmly established disposition (malaka) and a temporary state (haal).

Core Idea
What endures becomes character; what passes is merely a state.

Sanusi now divides the category of quality into its second branch: psychical qualities. These are not just any qualities; they are those found in living beings with souls. Moreover, they can be either stable or transient—a distinction that has implications for how we understand human character and even divine attributes. This passage presents that foundational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two main parts: a definition and a subdivision.
2
Note the special case of God's attributes
The author briefly mentions that those who affirm attributes for the Necessary Being do not consider them as ordinary qualities. This is a nuanced point that prevents confusion.
This shows that kalam uses these terms carefully and does not apply them univocally to God.
3
Understand the malaka/haal distinction
Focus on the criterion of firm establishment (rusukh). The same quality can shift from haal to malaka, just as a child becomes an old man. Reflect on why this distinction matters for human character.
The analogy of human growth illustrates that change does not imply difference in essence.

القسم الثاني : الكيفيات النفسانية أي المختصة بذوات الأنفس الحيوانية، وهي مع الرسوخ تسمى ملكة وبدونه حالاً . أي من الأقسام الأربعة للكيف الكيفيات المختصة بذوات الأنفس الحيوانية بمعنى أنها إنما تكون من بين الأجسام للحيوان دون النبات والجماد فلا يمتنع ثبوت بعضها لبعض المجردات من الواجب تعالى وغيره على أن القائلين بثبوت صفة الحياة والعلم والقدرة ونحوها للواجب لا يجعلونها من جنس الكيفيات والأعراض، ثم الكيفية النفسانية إن كانت راسخة سميت ملكة وإلا فحالاً ، فالتمايز بينهما قد لا يكون إلا بعارض بأن تكون الصفة حالاً ثم تصير بعينها ملكة، كما أن الشخص من الإنسان يكون صبياً ثم يصير شيخاً ومثل ذلك، وإن كان يسبق إلى الوهم ويقع في بعض
The second division: psychical qualities, i.e., those specific to animal souls, and when firmly established they are called 'malaka' (disposition) and without it 'haal' (state). That is, among the four divisions of quality, the qualities specific to animal souls, meaning that they exist among bodies only for animals, not for plants and inanimate objects. So it is not impossible to affirm some of them for some immaterial entities, such as the Necessary Exalted and others, although those who affirm the attributes of life, knowledge, power, and the like for the Necessary do not make them of the genus of qualities and accidents. Then, the psychical quality, if it is firmly established, is called 'malaka', otherwise 'haal'. So the distinction between them may be only by an accidental circumstance, such that the attribute is a 'haal' and then becomes the very same a 'malaka', just as a human being is a child and then becomes an old man, and similar to that, although it may occur to the imagination and fall in some...
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of quality (kayf) known as psychical qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-nafsaniyya). It explains that these qualities are specific to animal souls, meaning they are found in living beings with souls (animals) as opposed to plants and inanimate objects. The author then notes that some of these qualities may be attributed to immaterial entities, including the Necessary Being (God) and others, but those who affirm attributes like life, knowledge, and power for God do not classify them under the genus of qualities or accidents. The passage then introduces a crucial subdivision: a psychical quality, when firmly established and enduring, is called a 'malaka' (disposition or ingrained habit). If it is not firmly established and is transitory, it is called a 'haal' (state). The distinction between malaka and haal is not essential but accidental; the same quality can initially be a haal and later become a malaka through repetition and fixity, analogous to a child growing into an old man. The passage concludes with a phrase suggesting that this may seem counterintuitive at first, but it is a valid distinction.

Correction: The passage clarifies that it is not impossible to attribute some psychical qualities to God, though those who affirm divine attributes do not consider them as ordinary qualities or accidents.

Psychical qualities are divided into stable dispositions (malaka) and transient states (haal), with the same quality able to shift from one to the other through repetition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Are you treating your character as a mere collection of fleeting states, or are you consciously cultivating a malaka that reflects something of lasting excellence?

Psychical Qualities and the Disposition-State Distinction
The author defines psychical qualities and distinguishes between disposition and state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of psychical qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) and the distinction between a firmly established disposition (malaka) and a transient state (ḥāl).

Core Idea
Psychical qualities are specific to animal souls; their stability decides disposition vs. state.

This passage locates psychical qualities within the broader scheme of quality categories. The author clarifies their scope—they belong to animal souls, not to plants or inanimate objects, and they should not be confused with divine attributes. Finally, he introduces the critical distinction between a disposition and a state, based on permanence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the category
Recognize that psychical qualities are one of four divisions of quality (kayf). Read the opening phrase that places them as the second division.
This sets the context within Aristotelian categories.
2
Learn the definition
Note that psychical qualities are specific to animal souls and do not apply to divine attributes. The author emphasizes that even though God has attributes like life and knowledge, they are not of this genus.
3
Distinguish disposition from state
When a psychical quality is firmly established (rāsikha), it is called malaka (disposition); otherwise, it is ḥāl (state). This is the core distinction.
Permanence is the key factor.
4
Grasp the relativity
The same attribute can shift from state to disposition, like a human growing from child to adult. The author explains that the individual changes due to different individuating accidents.
This shows the distinction is based on change over time.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيفيات النفسانية
(قال : القسم الثاني : الكيفيات النفسانية أي المختصة بذوات الأنفس الحيوانية، وهي مع الرسوخ تسمى ملكة وبدونه حالاً .
أي من الأقسام الأربعة للكيف الكيفيات المختصة بذوات الأنفس الحيوانية بمعنى أنها إنما تكون من بين (۱) الأجسام للحيوان دون النبات والجماد فلا يمتنع ثبوت بعضها لبعض المجردات من الواجب تعالى وغيره على أن القائلين بثبوت صفة الحياة والعلم والقدرة ونحوها للواجب لا يجعلونها من جنس الكيفيات والأعراض، ثم الكيفية النفسانية إن كانت راسخة سميت ملكة (۲) وإلا فحالاً ، فالتمايز بينهما قد لا يكون إلا بعارض بأن تكون الصفة حالاً ثم تصير بعينها ملكة، كما أن الشخص من الإنسان يكون صبياً ثم يصير شيخاً ومثل ذلك، وإن كان يسبق إلى (٣) الوهم ويقع في بعض العبارات إنه هو ذلك الشخص بعينه فليس كذلك بحسب الحقيقة للقطع بتغاير العوارض المشخصة .
Psychical qualities. (He said: The second division: psychical qualities, i.e., those specific to animal souls, and with permanence they are called disposition, and without it, state. That is, among the four divisions of quality, the qualities specific to animal souls, meaning that they only occur among bodies for animals, not plants or inanimate objects. So it is not impossible to affirm some of them for certain immaterial beings, such as the Necessary (al-Wājib), exalted is He, and others, given that those who affirm the attributes of life, knowledge, power, and the like for the Necessary do not consider them to be of the genus of qualities and accidents. Then the psychical quality, if it is firmly established, is called disposition (malaka), otherwise state (ḥāl). The distinction between them may only be due to an accident, such that the attribute is a state and then becomes in itself a disposition, just as a human individual is a child and then becomes an old man, and the like. And although it occurs to the imagination and appears in some expressions that it is that same individual, it is not so in reality, because the individuating accidents are certainly different.)
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines psychical qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-nafsāniyya) as the second division of the four categories of quality. The author begins by stating that they are specific to animal souls, meaning they exist in animals but not plants or inanimate objects. He then clarifies that it is not impossible for some of these qualities to be affirmed for immaterial beings such as God, but those who affirm attributes like life, knowledge, and power for God do not consider them to be of the same genus as qualities and accidents—they are not psychical qualities in the same sense. Next, he explains that a psychical quality, if it is firmly established and enduring in the soul, is called a disposition (malaka), and if it is not enduring, it is called a state (ḥāl). The distinction between disposition and state is relative; the same attribute can initially be a state and later become a disposition through repetition and firmness, similar to how a person is a child and then becomes an old man—though in reality the individuating accidents change, so it is not the exact same individual. This illustrates that the attribute itself changes in terms of its fixity.

The Definition of Life and Its Three Interpretations
The author defines life and enumerates three interpretations with explanation of temperamental balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of life as a principle of sensation and movement, the three interpretations of life in bodies, and the concept of temperamental balance.

Core Idea
Life is known through its effects; its reality is beyond sensory expression.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explores the nature of life in physical bodies. The author begins with a clear definition: life is the principle behind sensation and movement. He then presents three interpretations that scholars have offered, emphasizing that they do not contradict but express the same reality through different lenses. The passage also introduces the concept of temperamental balance as a key condition for life.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to gain an overall sense.
Notice the author's concise style and the three sayings introduced by 'fa-qila'.
2
Grasp the definition of life
Focus on the statement 'life is a principle for the power of sensation and movement'. Understand that it is the source, not the power itself.
The example of the paralyzed limb helps illustrate this distinction.
3
Identify the three interpretations
Locate the three sayings (interpretations) and note how each one describes life from a different angle.
They are not conflicting; they highlight different aspects of the same reality.
4
Understand temperamental balance
Read the explanation of i'tidal al-mizaj and its three levels: specific, varietal, and individual.
This ties the definition of life to the physical composition of the body.
5
Reflect on the overall message
Ask yourself why the author distinguishes between the interpretations and what this teaches about theological methodology.
The key is that life is known through its effects, not directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحياة مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة قال : فمنها الحياة وهي في الشاهد قوة تقتضي الحس والحركة أي تكون مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة، وهذا معنى قولهم قوة تتبع اعتدال النوع وتفيض عنها سائر القوى أي القوى الحيوانية فتكون غير قوة الحس والحركة لتغاير المبدأ وذي المبدأ، وغير قوة التغذية لوجودها في النبات مع عدم الحياة، ولهذا كان في العضو المفلوج، أو الذابل الحياة بأثرها من غير حس وحركة أو اغتذاء . سيجيء معنى الحياة في حق الله تعالى وأما حياة الحي من الأجسام فقد اختلفت العبارات في تفسيرها لا من جهة اختلاف في حقيقتها، بل من جهة الاطلاع عليها والتعبير عنها إلا باعتبار اللوازم والآثار، فقيل : هي تقتضي الحس والحركة مشروطة باعتدال المزاج ، والقيد الأخير للتحقيق على ما رأي البعض لا للاحتراز وقيل قوة هي مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة، وكان هذا هو المراد بالأول ليتميز عن قوة الحس والحركة وقيل قوة تتبع اعتدال النوع ، ويفيض عنها سائر القوى الحيوانية أي المدركة والمحركة على ما تفصيلها ومعنى اعتدال النوع هو أن لكل نوع من المركبات العنصرية مزاجا خاصاً هو أصلح الأمزجة بالنسبة إليه بحيث إذا خرج عن ذلك المزاج لم يكن ذلك النوع ثم لكل صنف من ذلك النوع ، ولكل شخص من ذلك الصنف مزاج يخصه هو أصبح بالنسبة إليه ويسمى الأول اعتدالاً نوعياً والثاني صنفياً، والثالث شخصياً، ولهذا زيادة تفصيل وتحقيق يذكر في بحث المزاج
Life is a principle of the power of sensation and movement. He said: Among (the qualities) is life, and it is in the witnessed world a power requiring sensation and movement, i.e., it is a principle for the power of sensation and movement. This is the meaning of their saying: 'A power that follows the temperamental balance of the species and from which emanate the other powers,' i.e., the animal powers, so it is other than the power of sensation and movement due to the difference between the principle and that which has the principle, and other than the power of nutrition because it exists in plants without life. For this reason, life with its effect is present in a paralyzed limb without sensation and movement, or in a withered limb without nutrition. The meaning of life with respect to God Almighty will come later. As for the life of a living animal among bodies, the expressions differ concerning its interpretation, not due to a difference in its reality, but due to the mode of perceiving it and expressing it, only by considering its concomitants and effects. Some said: It requires sensation and movement conditioned by balanced temperament, and the last restriction is for verification according to some, not for exclusion. Some said: It is a power that is a principle for the power of sensation and movement, and this was intended by the first (interpretation) to distinguish it from the power of sensation and movement. Some said: It is a power that follows the temperamental balance of the species, and from it emanate the other animal powers, i.e., the perceptive and motive powers, according to their detailed explanation. The meaning of 'temperamental balance of the species' is that each species of elemental compounds has a specific temperament that is the most suitable temperament for it, such that if it departs from that temperament, that species does not exist. Then each variety of that species, and each individual of that variety, has a temperament particular to it that is the most suitable for it. The first is called specific temperamental balance (i'tidal naw'i), the second varietal, and the third individual. There is further detail and verification mentioned in the discussion of temperament.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines life (al-hayat) as a quality and explains its interpretation in physical bodies. It begins by stating the definition: life is a principle (mabda') for the power of sensation and movement. The commentary expands that in the witnessed world (shahid), life is a power requiring sensation and movement, i.e., it is the source from which the power of sensation and movement derives. The author notes that this is the meaning of the common saying that life is a power following the temperamental balance of the species (i'tidal al-naw'), from which emanate other animal powers. Life is distinct from both the power of sensation and the power of nutrition, because nutrition exists in plants without life. Evidence is given: a paralyzed limb (عضو مفلوج) has life (it does not decay) but no sensation or movement; a withered limb (عضو ذابل) has life but no nutrition. The author then notes that the meaning of life for God will come later. For animals, the various interpretations (اختلاف العبارات) are not about the essence of life but about how we perceive it through its effects. Three sayings are listed: (1) life requires sensation and movement conditioned by balanced temperament, with the condition being for verification not exclusion; (2) life is a power that is a principle for the power of sensation and movement, meant to distinguish it from that power; (3) life is a power that follows the temperamental balance of the species and from which emanate other animal powers (perceptive and motive). The meaning of temperamental balance (i'tidal al-naw') is then explained: each species has a best temperament, deviation destroys the species; then each variety and each individual has its own best temperament, called specific, varietal, and individual balance. Further details are in the discussion of temperament.

Correction: The passage states the differences are in expression, not in reality; they all refer to the same life through its effects.

?
You have a unique temperamental balance given by Allah. Are you living in harmony with your created nature, or are you forcing yourself into a mold that doesn't fit? What would it mean to embrace the specific balance Allah designed for you, both physically and spiritually?

The Three Levels of Temperament Equilibrium
Sanusi defines three types of temperament equilibrium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three hierarchical levels of temperament equilibrium (specific, varietal, individual) and why each is necessary for the existence of species, varieties, and individuals.

Core Idea
Temperament equilibrium is relative: each species, variety, and individual has its own optimal balance.

Before discussing how life emerges from the body, Sanusi clarifies what 'temperament equilibrium' (i'tidal al-mizaj) truly means. He explains that it is not a single universal balance but a three-tiered concept: what is balanced for a species may differ for its varieties, and even for each individual. Understanding these levels is essential for grasping how life and faculties are conditioned by the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on equilibrium
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines 'specific equilibrium' (i'tidal naw'i) and then extends it to varietal and individual levels.
The passage uses a very precise classification scheme.
2
Identify the three levels
Identify the names of the three levels: specific (naw'i), varietal (sinafi), and individual (shakhsi). Understand what each level refers to: species, variety, person.
Think of examples: human species, ethnic groups, specific persons.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi start with equilibrium before discussing life? How does this classification set the stage for the argument about life being distinct from faculties?
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سيجيء ويفيض عنها سائر القوى الحيوانية أي المدركة والمحركة على ما تفصيلها ومعنى اعتدال النوع هو أن لكل نوع من المركبات العنصرية مزاجا خاصاً هو أصلح الأمزجة بالنسبة إليه بحيث إذا خرج عن ذلك المزاج لم يكن ذلك النوع ثم لكل صنف من ذلك النوع ، ولكل شخص من ذلك الصنف مزاج يخصه هو أصبح بالنسبة إليه ويسمى الأول اعتدالاً نوعياً والثاني صنفياً، والثالث شخصياً، ولهذا زيادة تفصيل وتحقيق يذكر في بحث المزاج فإذا حصل في المركب اعتدال يليق بنوع من أنواع الحيوان فاض عليه قوة الحياة فانبعثت عنها بإذن الله تعالى الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة والقوى المحركة نحو جلب المنافع ودفع المضار فتكون الحياة مشروطة باعتدال المزاج، ومبدأ القوة الحس والحركة فتغاير هما بالضرورة وكذا تغاير القوة الغاذية لوجودها في النبات بخلاف الحياة لكن هذا إنما يتم لو ثبت أن الحياة مبدأ لقوة الحس والحركة لا نفسها وأن الغاذية في النبات والحيوان حقيقة واحدة ليلزم من مغايرة تلك الحياة مغايرة هذه لها فاستدلوا على مغايرة الحياة لقوة الحس والحركة ولقوة التغذية الحيوانية بأن الحياة موجودة في العضو المفلوج للحيوان من غير حس وحركة ، وفي العضو الذابل من غير اغتذاء . واعترض الإمام بأن عدم الإحساس والحركة وعدم الاغتذاء لا يدلان على عدم قوة الحس والحركة وعدم قوة التغذية لجواز أن توجد القوة ولا يصدر عنها الأثر لمانع من جهة القابل . وأجيب بأن القوة ما يصدر عنه الأثر بالفعل بمعنى أنا نريد أن القوة التي تصدر عنها بالفعل آثار الحياة كحفظ العضو عن التعفن مثلاً ،باقية، والقوة التي يصدر عنها بالفعل الحس والحركة والتغذية غير باقية فلا تكون هي هي بهذا يشعر كلام تلخيص المحصل وليس معناه أن القوة اسم لما يصدر عنه الأثر بالفعل فإن ظاهر البطلان كيف وهو قد صرح بأن في العضو المفلوج قوة الحس والحركة باقية لكنها عاجزة عن الإحساس والحركة. نعم يتوجه يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون مبدأ جميع تلك الآثار قوة واحدة هي الحياة وقد يعجز عن البعض دون البعض لخصوصية المانع لكن الحق أن مغايرة المعنى المسمى بالحياة للقوة الباصرة والسامعة وغيرهما من القوى الحيوانية والطبيعية مما لا يحتاج إلى البيان .
It will come and the rest of the animal powers, i.e., the perceptive and motive, emanate from it, according to their detailed explanation. And the meaning of specific equilibrium is that for every species of elemental compounds there is a particular temperament that is the most suitable temperament for it, such that if it departs from that temperament, that species does not exist. Then for every variety of that species, and for every individual of that variety, there is a temperament specific to it that is the most suitable for it. The first is called specific equilibrium, the second varietal, and the third individual. There is further detail and verification mentioned in the discussion of temperament. When a suitable equilibrium for a species of animals occurs in a compound, the power of life emanates upon it, and by the permission of God the external and internal senses and the motive powers for attracting benefits and repelling harms arise from it. Thus life is conditioned by equilibrium of temperament. And it (life) is the principle of the power of sense and movement, so they necessarily differ from it, and likewise the nutritive power differs from it because it exists in plants unlike life. But this is only valid if it is established that life is the principle of the power of sense and movement, not they themselves, and that the nutritive (power) in plants and animals is one reality, so that the difference of life from those forces entails the difference of this (the nutritive) from them. They argued for the difference of life from the power of sense and movement and from the animal nutritive power by the fact that life exists in a paralyzed limb of an animal without sense and movement, and in a withered limb without nutrition. The Imam objected that the absence of sensation and movement and the absence of nutrition do not indicate the absence of the power of sense and movement and the absence of the power of nutrition, because it is possible that the power exists but its effect does not issue due to an obstacle from the receiver. It was answered that power is that from which the effect actually issues, meaning that we mean that the power from which the effects of life actually issue, like preserving the limb from decay, remains, while the power from which sense, movement, and nutrition actually issue does not remain, so they are not the same. This is what is indicated by the words of Talkhis al-Muhassal, and its meaning is not that power is a name for that from which the effect actually issues, for that is clearly false—how could it be, when he has stated that in the paralyzed limb the power of sense and movement remains but is incapable of sensation and movement. Yes, it may be said: why is it not permissible that the principle of all these effects is one power, which is life, and it may fail to produce some effects while producing others due to the specificity of the obstacle? But the truth is that the difference between the meaning called life and the powers of sight, hearing, and other animal and natural powers does not need demonstration.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument that begins by defining the concept of temperament equilibrium (i'tidal al-mizaj) at three levels: specific (naw'i), varietal (sinafi), and individual (shakhsi). It then describes how when a suitable equilibrium occurs in a compound, life emanates and then the external and internal senses and motive powers arise. The author then asserts that life is the principle (mabda') of the powers of sense and movement, and therefore necessarily distinct from them. Similarly, the nutritive power is distinct because it exists in plants, whereas life does not. This is proven by the example of a paralyzed limb (which has life but no sense/movement) and a withered limb (which has life but no nutrition). An objection by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) follows: the absence of effects does not prove the absence of the power because a hindrance could exist. The answer is that the power is defined as that which actually produces its effect; the power that preserves the limb from decay (life) remains, while the power that produces sense, movement, and nutrition does not, showing they are distinct. The passage then clarifies that this does not mean power is named only after actual effect, citing Talkhis al-Muhassal. Finally, it raises a possible objection that all effects could come from a single life power, but dismisses it, stating that the distinction of life from the powers of sight, hearing, etc., is self-evident.

?
You often neglect your body's needs—sleep, food, rest—as unimportant. Yet this lesson teaches that your very soul's powers (perception and motivation) depend on a balanced temperament. What will you change starting tomorrow to protect this gift from Allah?

Proof That Life Is Distinct from the Faculties
Sanusi proves life is distinct from faculties using empirical evidence and addressing an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that life is a distinct principle from the powers of sense, movement, and nutrition, supported by the examples of a paralyzed limb and a withered limb, and how an objection and its answer refine the proof.

Core Idea
Life can exist without sensation, movement, or nutrition, proving it is a different principle.

Having established the role of temperament, Sanusi now argues that life (qūwat al-ḥayāt) is not identical to the faculties of sense, movement, or nutrition. He uses observable phenomena—a paralyzed limb that still lives but lacks sensation and movement, and a withered limb that lives but lacks nutrition—to demonstrate their distinction. An objection from Imam al-Razi and the subsequent answer deepen the argument, showing the subtlety of the concept of 'power' in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and examples
Read the passage from 'When a suitable equilibrium occurs' through the examples of the paralyzed and withered limbs. Notice the claim: life is the principle of sense/movement, so they differ.
The examples are empirical and central to the proof.
2
Understand al-Razi's objection
Read the objection: absence of effect does not prove absence of power, because a hindrance could exist. This is a logical challenge to the argument.
This objection is typical in kalam reasoning.
3
Examine the response
Read the answer: power is defined as that which actually issues its effect. The power of life (preserving from decay) remains, while the power for sense/movement/nutrition does not, showing they are distinct.
The response refines what is meant by 'power'.
4
Consider the final clarification
Read the final part where the author dismisses the possibility of a single principle (life) for all effects, stating the distinction is self-evident.
This shows the author's confidence in the conclusion.

It will come and the rest of the animal powers, i.e., the perceptive and motive, emanate from it, according to their detailed explanation. And the meaning of specific equilibrium is that for every species of elemental compounds there is a particular temperament that is the most suitable temperament for it, such that if it departs from that temperament, that species does not exist. Then for every variety of that species, and for every individual of that variety, there is a temperament specific to it that is the most suitable for it. The first is called specific equilibrium, the second varietal, and the third individual. There is further detail and verification mentioned in the discussion of temperament. When a suitable equilibrium for a species of animals occurs in a compound, the power of life emanates upon it, and by the permission of God the external and internal senses and the motive powers for attracting benefits and repelling harms arise from it. Thus life is conditioned by equilibrium of temperament. The Reality of Perception
Definition of perception as presence to the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the reality of perception (idrak) is the presence of the thing to the intellect, not its external existence, and the different ways this presence occurs.

Core Idea
Perception is the intellectual presence of a thing, not its physical existence.

The First Investigation of this section explores the fundamental nature of perception. Before discussing theological or philosophical implications, the author establishes a precise definition: perception is the presence of the perceived thing to the intellect. This definition avoids common confusions and sets the stage for further analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the main claim. Notice the rejection of external existence as the criterion for perception.
The key phrase: 'فحقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل.'
2
Understand the two types of presence
The passage mentions that presence can be by the thing itself or by its form. Consider examples: direct intellectual apprehension vs. abstracted forms.
Also note the reference to Avicenna's al-Isharat.
3
Examine the footnote on usage
Read the footnote to see linguistic and philosophical distinctions, especially between particular and universal perception.
This deepens your understanding of the term.
4
Articulate the core idea
Summarize in your own words: perception is not about external existence but intellectual presence. Why does this matter for theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : لا خفاء أنا إذا أدركنا شيئاً كان له تميز وظهور عند العقل، وليس ذلك بوجوده العيني إذ كثيراً ما يدرك ولا وجود أو يوجد ولا إدراك، بل بوجوده العقلي، وهو المعنى بالصورة، فحقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل.
أي من الكيفيات النفسانية الإدراك، وقد سبق نبذ من الكلام فيه، والذي استقر عليه رأي المحققين من الفلاسفة أن حقيقة إدراك الشيء حضوره عند العقل، إما بنفسه وإما بصورته المنتزعة، أو الحاصلة ابتداء المرتسمة في العقل الذي هو المدرك أو آلته التي بها الإدراك، وهذا معنى ما قال في الإشارات إدراك الشيء هو أن تكون حقيقته متمثلة عند المدرك يشاهدها ما به يدرك، على أن المراد بتمثل الحقيقة حضورها بنفسها أو بمثالها سواء كان
(١) الإدراك في اللغة هو اللحاق والوصول يقال : أدرك الشيء بلغ وقته وانتهى، وأدرك الثمر نضج، وأدرك الولد بلغ وأدرك الشيء لحقه، وأدرك المسألة علمها ، وأدرك الشيء البصره رآه ، فمن رأى شيئاً ورأى جوانبه ونهاياته قيل: إنه أدركه، ويصح : رأيت الحبيب، وما أدركه بصري، فيكون الإدراك بهذا المعنى أخص من الرؤية. والإدراك عند معظم الفلاسفة إما أن يكون إدراك الجزئي أو إدراك الكلي وإدراك الجزئي قد يكون بحيث يتوقف على وجوده في الخارج، وهو الحس، أو لا يتوقف وهو الخيال، وإدراك الجزئي على وجه كلي هو إدراك الكلية الذي ينحصر في ذلك الجزئي . راجع لباب الاشارات للرازي ص ٧٤ .
The First Investigation: It is no secret that when we perceive something, it has distinction and manifestation to the intellect. That is not by its external existence, for often something is perceived and has no existence, or exists and is not perceived, but rather by its intellectual existence, which is meant by 'the form'. So the reality of perception of a thing is its presence to the intellect. That is, perception is one of the psychical qualities, and some discussion of it has preceded. What the opinion of the verifying philosophers has settled upon is that the reality of perception of a thing is its presence to the intellect, either by itself or by its abstracted form, or the form initially acquired and impressed in the intellect which is the perceiver or the instrument by which perception occurs. This is the meaning of what was said in al-Isharat: 'Perception of a thing is that its reality be represented to the perceiver, the means of perception witnessing it,' with the understanding that the representation of the reality is its presence by itself or by its likeness, whether it be (1) Perception in language is attainment and arrival. It is said: a thing 'adraka' means its time came and ended; fruit 'adraka' means ripened; a child 'adraka' means reached maturity; one 'adraka' a thing means overtook it; one 'adraka' a problem means understood it; one's sight 'adraka' a thing means saw it. So whoever sees a thing and sees its sides and ends, it is said he 'adraka' it, and it is correct to say 'I saw the beloved but my sight did not perceive him,' thus perception in this sense is more specific than vision. And perception according to most philosophers is either perception of the particular or perception of the universal. Perception of the particular may be such that it depends on its existence in the external, which is sense perception, or does not depend, which is imagination. And perception of the particular in a universal manner is perception of the universality that is confined to that particular. See the margin of al-Isharat by al-Razi, p. 74.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage defines the reality of perception (idrak) and explains its nature according to the verifying philosophers. The author begins by stating that when we perceive something, it becomes distinct and manifest to the intellect. This manifestation is not due to the thing's external existence (al-wujud al-'ayni), because often we perceive something that does not exist in the external world (like imaginary objects), or something exists but is not perceived. Rather, the perception is by the thing's intellectual existence (al-wujud al-'aqli), which is called 'the form' (al-surah). Thus, the essential definition: 'Perception is presence of the thing to the intellect.' This is the first investigation. The author then explains that perception is one of the psychical qualities (al-kayfiyyat al-nafsaniyya), and some discussion of it preceded. The settled view of the verifying philosophers is that perception is presence to the intellect, either by the thing itself (bi nafsihi) — meaning direct intellectual apprehension, or by its abstracted form (bi suratihi al-muntaza'ah), which is the form derived from the external object, or the form initially acquired (al-hasilah ibtida'an) and impressed in the intellect (al-murtasimah fi al-'aql), where the intellect is the perceiver (al-mudrik) or its instrument (alatuhu). Then the author cites Avicenna's al-Isharat: 'Perception of a thing is that its reality be represented (mutamaththilah) to the perceiver, witnessed by that by which it perceives,' meaning that the representation of the reality is its presence either by itself or by its likeness (mithal). The passage then includes a footnote (1) that provides linguistic and philosophical definitions of idrak. Linguistically, idrak means attainment (al-lahaq) and arrival (al-wusul). Various usages are given: a thing's time coming and ending, fruit ripening, a child maturing, overtaking, understanding a problem, seeing a thing and comprehending its sides and ends. Thus, idrak in this sense is more specific than vision (ru'yah). Philosophically, according to most philosophers, idrak is either perception of the particular (juz'i) or perception of the universal (kulli). Perception of the particular may depend on external existence (sense perception, al-hiss) or not depend (imagination, al-khayal). Perception of the particular in a universal manner is perception of the universality confined to that particular. The footnote ends with a reference to al-Razi's commentary on al-Isharat. The passage thus establishes a precise technical definition of perception, distinguishing it from mere external existence and linking it to intellectual presence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is the same as the external existence of a thing.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that perception is not by external existence (al-wujud al-'ayni), because often something is perceived without existing externally, or exists without being perceived.

Checkpoint

C) Sensory vision of the thing.
D) The act of knowing a universal concept.

Key Takeaway
The reality of perception is the presence of the thing to the intellect, either by itself or by its form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think about things that have no physical existence — like your own future, the meaning of justice, or even Allah. According to this lesson, those thoughts are real perceptions if they are present to your intellect. Does this change how you value your inner thoughts compared to your outer possessions? What one non-physical thing will you deliberately bring to your intellect today, with full presence?
?
Have you ever realized that your perception of another person is not just their physical appearance but an intellectual presence in your mind? How might this lesson transform the way you interact with someone you find difficult? Make a resolve tonight to pause before reacting, and consider what intellectual presence of that person you carry within you.
?
If perception can occur without physical existence, then your relationship with Allah is truly based on intellectual presence. Is He really present in your mind when you think of Him? What would change in your daily life if you deliberately maintained that presence? Set a specific time tomorrow to sit and focus solely on the intellectual presence of Allah.

Answering the Circularity Objection in Defining Idrak
The author responds to the objection that defining idrak involves circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of circularity in defining idrak is answered and why the definition is not actually circular.

Core Idea
Not all definitions that use related terms are circular—context determines circularity.

This passage addresses a common logical objection: that defining perception (idrak) using 'the perceived' (mudrak) creates a vicious circle. The author provides two distinct responses, showing that either the terms are understood referentially or the statement is not a strict definition at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the objection and the two answers.
Pay attention to the structure: objection then two answers.
2
Identify the objection
Note the claim that mentioning 'mudrak' and 'ma bihi yudrak' in the definition creates circularity.
This is a common logical fallacy called dawr.
3
Analyze the first answer
Understand that the author clarifies that 'mudrak' refers to the thing perceived, not a term requiring definition, so no circle occurs.
Even if the exact nature of that thing is unknown, the reference avoids circularity.
4
Analyze the second answer
See that the statement is not a formal definition but a designation (ta'yin) and summary of a meaning clear to the intellect.
This is a stronger response: the objection is irrelevant if it's not a definition.
5
Connect to broader kalam
Reflect on how this defense preserves the clarity of the concept of idrak without falling into logical error.
Understanding this helps in analyzing other definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض بأن ذكر المدرك وما به يدرك في تعريف الإدراك دور، فجوابه أن المراد به الشيء الذي قال له : المدرك وما به الإدراك، وإن لم تعرف حقيقة هذا الوصف، وقد يجاب بأن هذا ليس تعريفاً للإدراك، بل تعييناً وتلخيصاً للمعنى المسمى بالإدراك الواضح عند العقل .
The objection that mentioning 'the perceived' (al-mudrak) and 'that by which it is perceived' (ma bihi yudrak) in the definition of perception (al-idrak) is a circle (dawr). Its answer is that what is meant by it is the thing which is said to be the perceived and that by which perception occurs, even if the reality of this description is not known. And it may also be answered that this is not a definition (ta‘rif) of perception, but rather a designation and summary of the meaning called perception, which is clear to the intellect.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to a definition of perception (idrak) and gives two responses. The objection states that using 'the perceived' (mudrak) and 'that by which it is perceived' (ma bihi yudrak) in the definition creates a vicious circle (dawr). The first answer clarifies that the term 'mudrak' here refers to the thing that is known through perception, not to a term that itself requires definition; thus no circularity occurs even if the exact nature of that thing is not known. The second answer goes further, arguing that what is being given is not a strict definition (ta‘rif) but rather a designation (ta‘yin) and summary (talkhis) of a meaning that is already intuitively clear to the intellect. Thus the objection is resolved in two ways: either the terms are understood reference-wise, or the statement is not a definition at all.

Key Terms
إدراك
مدرك
دور
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mention of the same root in a definition is automatically circular.
Correction: If the term is used in a known sense (referentially) without requiring definition, or if the statement is not a strict definition, circularity is avoided.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer given to the objection that defining idrak with 'mudrak' and 'ma bihi yudrak' is circular?
A) The definition is actually a proper definition and the circle is harmless.
B) The term 'mudrak' refers to the thing known, not to the definition itself, so no circle occurs.
C) The objection is invalid because idrak cannot be defined.
D) The definition is taken from revelation and cannot be questioned.

Key Takeaway
The objection of circularity in defining idrak is refuted by either interpreting the terms referentially or by denying that the statement is a strict definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain your faith to others, do you ever find yourself using terms that depend on each other? How does this lesson's distinction between definition and designation help you present tawhid clearly without falling into circular reasoning?
?
The second response says the meaning of idrak is 'clear to the intellect.' Is your understanding of your Lord's attributes equally clear? What prevents that clarity from settling in your heart during moments of doubt?
?
If someone accused you of circular reasoning when you affirmed that the Qur'an is true because it is the word of Allah, and that Allah exists because the Qur'an says so, how would you apply the two responses from this passage to defend your position without compromising on truth?

Three Kinds of Perceived Things
The author divides the perceived thing into three kinds based on its relation to the perceiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of perceived things based on their relation to the perceiver, and why abstraction is not always necessary for perception.

Core Idea
Perception is either of the self directly, or of external things via abstraction or direct mental images.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a fundamental epistemological classification. The author distinguishes between perception of one's own essence, perception of material objects through abstraction, and perception of immaterial or non-existent things through non-abstracted images. Understanding this division clarifies how different types of objects are known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the initial division into external and non-external.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three types of perception: by essence (self-perception), by abstracted image (material objects), and by non-abstracted image (immaterial/non-existent).
The text mentions examples for each.
3
Understand the conceptual distinction
Note that in self-perception, the distinction between perceiver and perceived is only conceptual (i‘tibari), not real. This avoids infinite regress.
The example of a healer healing himself illustrates this.
4
Reflect on examples
Think about the soul perceiving itself, the perception of a material object (like a tree), and the perception of an immaterial being or a non-existent (like a vacuum).
These examples show how the classification works.
5
Connect to broader epistemology
Consider how this classification sets the foundation for discussions about knowledge of God, angels, and other metaphysical entities.
This passage is a building block for later topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو المدرك إما أن يكون خارجاً أو غير خارج (قال: أما بحقيقته كإدراك النفس ذاتها وصفاتها فيكون التغاير اعتبارياً كاف كالمعالج يعالج نفسه، ومثله العلم بالعلم فلا يلزم وجود ما لا يتناهى وإما بصورته المنتزعة كما في الماديات أو غير المنتزعة كما في المجردات والمعدومات). إشارة إلى ما ذكروا من أن الشيء المدرك إما أن لا يكون خارجاً عن ذات المدرك كالنفس ،وصفاتها، وإما أن يكون خارجاً وحينئذ فإما أن يكون مادياً أو غير مادي . فالأول تكون حقيقته المتمثلة عند المدرك نفس حقيقته الموجودة في الخارج فيكون إدراكه دائماً . والثاني : تكون صورة منتزعة عنه . والثالث : تكون صورة متحصلة في العقل غير مفتقرة إلى الانتزاع من حقيقة خارجية، لكونها صورة لما هو مجرد في نفسه كإدراك المفارقات، أو لما لا تحقق له في الخارج ولا حقيقة أصلاً كإدراك المعـدومات.
And the perceived (al-mudrak) is either external or non-external. (He said: As for by its essence, like the soul's perception of itself and its attributes, then the distinction (taghayyur) is conceptual (i‘tibari) and sufficient, like the healer healing himself; similarly, knowledge of knowledge, so an infinite regress does not necessarily occur. And as for by its abstracted image (suratuhu al-muntaza‘a) as in material things, or non-abstracted as in immaterial things and non-existents.) This is an indication of what they mentioned: that the perceived thing either is not external to the essence of the perceiver, like the soul and its attributes, or is external. Then if external, it is either material or immaterial. The first: its reality (haqiqa) represented before the perceiver is the very same reality existing externally, so its perception is perpetual. The second: it is an image abstracted from it. The third: it is an image obtained in the mind that does not require abstraction from an external reality, because it is an image of what is immaterial in itself, like perception of separate substances (mufariqat), or of what has no realization externally and no reality at all, like perception of non-existents (ma‘dumat).
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage establishes a threefold classification of the perceived thing (mudrak) based on its relation to the perceiver. The author begins by stating the division into external and non-external. Then a quotation (likely from an earlier authority) explains the three types. The first type is perception by its essence (bi-haqiqatihi), exemplified by the soul's perception of itself and its attributes. Here, the distinction between perceiver and perceived is merely conceptual (i‘tibari), not real, so no infinite regress results. The second type is perception via an abstracted image (sura muntaza‘a), as in material things where the form is abstracted from the external object. The third type is perception via a non-abstracted image, which applies to immaterial entities (mujarradat) and non-existents (ma‘dumat). The author then elaborates: if the perceived is the perceiver's own essence (like the soul), its reality is present to itself perpetually. If external and material, an image is abstracted. If external and immaterial or non-existent, an image exists in the mind without needing abstraction. This classification sets up the epistemological basis for discussing how different objects are known.

خارج
حقيقة
صورة
منتزعة
مجردات
معدومات
تغاير اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that abstraction always occurs in perception.
Correction: The author shows that self-perception and perception of immaterial things do not involve abstraction; they use either direct presence (by essence) or non-abstracted images.

Checkpoint
According to the division, how is the soul's perception of itself categorized?
A) By an abstracted image
B) By its essence, with only a conceptual distinction
C) As external and material
D) As non-existent

Key Takeaway
The perceived is either the perceiver's own essence, an abstraction from a material object, or a non-abstracted image of an immaterial or non-existent thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly know your own soul directly, or have you been more preoccupied with external things, neglecting self-awareness? How will you cultivate a deeper, direct perception of your inner self today?
?
If you can conceive of things that do not exist (like a fictional story), does that not indicate that your mind is not limited to material abstractions? What does this tell you about the nature of your own intellect and its capacity for the unseen?
?
Since the soul's perception of itself is perpetual and immediate, what excuse do you have for remaining ignorant of your own faults and spiritual state? Will you set aside time each day for honest self-reflection, using this direct knowledge?
