First Objection: Perpetual Awareness
First objection to self-perception and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the soul's self-perception (that it would be perpetual) and the author's answer that denies the premises of the consequent.

Core Idea
Self-perception does not imply perpetual conscious awareness of all attributes.

The claim that the soul perceives itself by its essence faces several objections. The first argues that if self-perception is by presence, then the soul must be perpetually aware of all its attributes—yet we only know many attributes after reflection. This lesson examines this objection and the author's response, which denies the premises that lead to the false consequent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the first objection and its answer. Notice how the objection is structured: if self-perception is by presence, then perpetual awareness follows; but we are not always aware, so the consequent is false.
2
Understand the reasoning
The objector claims that since the soul is always present to itself, it must always be aware of all its attributes. But we only become aware after reflection, so the premise is flawed.
Consider whether this objection is convincing.
3
Examine the answer
The author answers by denying the premises that lead to the falsity of the consequent, calling this denial 'obstinacy' (mukābara). Reflect on whether this is a strong response.
The author does not provide a detailed refutation, simply rejects the premise.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
This objection is the first of three. The answer here is brief, but the following lesson will cover the stronger objections and more robust answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على الأول بوجوه : أحدها: أنه يقتضي أن يكون إدراك النفس لذاتها وصفاتها دائماً لدوام الحضور، واللازم باطل لأن كثيراً من الصفات مما لا نطلع على أنيتها وماهيتها إلا بعد النظر والتأمل، وإنما الكلام في ماهية النفس، ولا يجوز أن يكون هذا ذهولاً عن العلم بالعلم، لأنه أيضاً مما يلزم دوامه سيما وهم يقولون : إن علمنا بذاتنا نفس ذاتنا . وثانيها: أن حصول الشيء للشيء وحضوره عنده يقتضي تغاير الشيئين ضرورة فيمتنع علم الشيء بنفسه. وثالثها: أن النفس إذا كانت عالمة بذاتها وصفاتها كانت عالمة بعلمها بذلك، وهلم جراً لا إلى نهاية فيلزم علوم غير متناهية بالفعل، وأجيب عن الأول بمنع مقدمات بطلان اللازم وهو مكابرة وعن الأخيرين بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف والاعتبارات العقلية تنقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار. وحاصله أن ليس هناك إلا شيء واحد هو ذلك المجرد المدرك وهو ليس بغائب عن نفسه، فمن حيث يعتبر شاهداً يكون عالماً. ومن حيث يعتبر مشهوداً يكون معلوماً ، ومن حيث يعتبر مشهودا يكون علماً، ومرجعه إلى أن وجود الشيء عين حصوله، وحضوره لا يزيد عليه بحسب الخارج . المدرك لا يتصف بما يدركه لتغاير الصورة والهوية قال : ولما بين صورة الشيء وهويته من التغاير لم يلزم اتصاف المدرك بما يدركه من السواد والحرارة والاستدارة ونحو ذلك على أن حصول الصورة للنفس ليس كحصول العرض للجوهر ولهذا لا يلزم من إدراك المعاني العقلية أيضاً كالإيمان والكفر والجود والبخل اتصاف النفس بها ، وإنما الكلام في عكسه فكيف في مثل حصول السواد والبياض للجسم، ثم لا يقدح في ذلك أن المبصر هو هذا السواد لا شبحه والمتعقل هو الإنسان لا
And objections were raised against the first [type] in several ways. First: that it necessitates that the soul's perception of itself and its attributes be perpetual due to continuous presence, but the consequent is false because many of the attributes we do not become aware of their essence and quiddity except after reflection and contemplation, and the discussion is about the essence of the soul, and it is not permissible that this be forgetfulness of knowledge of knowledge, because that too would necessitate its perpetuity, especially since they say that our knowledge of our essence is our very essence. Second: that the occurrence of a thing for a thing and its presence before it necessitates a distinction between the two things necessarily, so a thing knowing itself is impossible. Third: that if the soul knows itself and its attributes, it knows its knowledge of that, and so on ad infinitum, necessitating actually infinite knowledges. And the first was answered by denying the premises of the falsity of the consequent, which is obstinacy. And the latter two were answered by [saying] that the conceptual distinction (al-taghayyur al-i‘tibari) is sufficient, and intellectual considerations cease with the cessation of consideration. The summary is that there is nothing except one entity, which is that abstract perceiver, and it is not absent from itself; so from the perspective it is considered as witness, it is knower; from the perspective it is considered as witnessed, it is known; and from the perspective it is considered as witnessed, it is knowledge. Its return is that the existence of a thing is identical to its occurrence, and its presence does not add anything to it according to externality. The perceiver is not qualified by what it perceives due to the distinction between image (sura) and identity (huwiyya). He said: Since there is a distinction between the image of a thing and its identity, it does not necessitate that the perceiver be qualified by what it perceives, such as blackness, heat, roundness, and the like. Moreover, the occurrence of an image in the soul is not like the occurrence of an accident in a substance. Therefore, from perceiving intellectual meanings like faith, disbelief, generosity, and stinginess, it does not necessitate that the soul be qualified by them. The discussion is about its opposite, so how about in the case of blackness and whiteness occurring in a body? Then it does not detract from that that the seen object is this blackness, not its phantom, and the intellected is the human being, not...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the claim that the soul perceives itself by its essence, followed by answers and a concluding clarification. The first objection argues that if the soul always perceives itself, it would be perpetually aware of all its attributes, which is false because many attributes are only known after reflection. The author answers this by denying the premise that the soul is always aware of all attributes – awareness may be potential, not actual. The second objection states that for one thing to know another, there must be a real distinction, making self-knowledge impossible. The third objection posits an infinite regress: if the soul knows itself, it knows that it knows, and so on. The author answers the last two together by invoking 'conceptual distinction' (taghayyur i‘tibari): the knower and known are the same entity, distinguished only mentally, and such mental distinctions do not generate actual multiplicity. The summary clarifies that the soul is a single abstract entity which, considered as witness, is knower; considered as witnessed, is known; and considered as witnessed, is also knowledge – the existence of the thing is identical to its presence. A further point is then made: the perceiver is not qualified by the attributes of the perceived object because the image (sura) of the thing in the mind is distinct from its reality (huwiyya) in the external world. Thus, perceiving blackness does not make the soul black, just as perceiving faith does not make the soul faithful. This holds even for sensible qualities, where the object seen is the actual blackness, not its mere phantom. Therefore, the objections are fully resolved, reinforcing the doctrine of self-perception without absurd consequences.

Key Terms
نفس
صفات
حضور
ذهول
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-perception means the soul is always fully conscious of all its attributes.
Correction: The soul may have potential knowledge; actual awareness requires reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, why is self-perception problematic?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
B) Because it would require perpetual awareness of all attributes, which is false.
C) Because it requires a real distinction between knower and known.
D) Because the soul cannot know itself at all.

Key Takeaway
The first objection to self-perception is that it would entail perpetual awareness; the author responds by denying the premises, though the answer is weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you claim to know yourself, when was the last time you discovered a hidden flaw you had ignored for years? What does that say about your claim to self-awareness?
?
Are you content to remain oblivious to your deeper attributes, or are you willing to confront the uncomfortable truths about yourself that only reflection reveals?
?
The author calls his own answer 'obstinacy.' How often do you obstinately cling to comfortable self-images rather than admit the full reality of who you are?

Second and Third Objections: Real Distinction and Infinite Regress
Second and third objections to self-perception and their answer, plus clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third objections to self-perception (requiring real distinction and leading to infinite regress) and the author's answer using conceptual distinction, as well as the clarification that the perceiver is not qualified by the perceived.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction saves self-perception from requiring real separation or infinite regress.

Beyond the perpetual awareness objection, two more powerful criticisms are raised: self-knowledge would require a real distinction between knower and known, leading to the impossibility of a thing knowing itself, and would generate an infinite chain of knowledges. This lesson presents these objections and the author's elegant solution: a conceptual distinction (taghayyur i'tibari) suffices. The lesson also addresses the further point that the soul is not qualified by the attributes it perceives.

Guided Path
1
Read the second and third objections
Read the Arabic passage from 'وثانيها' to 'في الإنسان لا'. Identify the second objection (real distinction) and third objection (infinite regress).
2
Understand the reasoning behind each objection
The second objection says that for one thing to be present to another, they must be distinct; self-presence would violate this. The third objection says knowing oneself leads to a chain of knowledge without end.
3
Study the author's unified answer
The author answers both by invoking conceptual distinction (taghayyur i‘tibari): the knower and known are the same entity, distinguished only mentally. Intellectual considerations stop when consideration stops.
This prevents infinite regress.
4
Examine the summary and clarification
The passage then summarizes: the soul is one entity, considered as knower, known, and knowledge. Then it clarifies that the soul is not qualified by what it perceives because the image (sura) is distinct from the identity (huwiyya) of the thing.

Thus, perceiving blackness does not make the soul black, just as perceiving faith does not make the soul faithful. This holds even for sensible qualities, where the object seen is the actual blackness, not its mere phantom. Therefore, the objections are fully resolved, reinforcing the doctrine of self-perception without absurd consequences.

Correction: The image in the soul is distinct from the identity of the thing; the soul does not become black by perceiving blackness.

Key Takeaway
The soul's self-perception is saved by a conceptual distinction: the same entity is knower, known, and knowledge under different considerations, and the soul is not qualified by the images it perceives.

?

Three Objections to Self-Knowledge and Their Answer
Author enumerates three objections to self-knowledge and answers with conceptual distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three classic objections raised against the soul's self-knowledge and how they are refuted by invoking a conceptual (not real) distinction between knower, known, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is possible because the soul is one reality considered from different aspects.

This passage presents a pivotal moment in the discussion of self-knowledge. The author lists three objections that challenge the possibility of the soul knowing itself. Each objection raises a different logical concern: circularity, the need for distinction, and infinite regress. The answer elegantly resolves these by pointing out that the distinction between knower and known is not real but conceptual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: three objections followed by an answer. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
The first objection is embedded in the opening line.
2
Identify each objection
Locate the three objections: the first is about identity of knowledge and essence, the second about the need for distinction, the third about infinite regress.
3
Understand the answer
Note how the author dismisses the first objection as stubbornness, then answers the last two with 'conceptual distinction suffices'. Grasp what 'conceptual distinction' means here.
The answer avoids both real separation and infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the soul is one abstract perceiver that is knower, known, and knowledge from different aspects. Appreciate how this solves the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنيتها وماهيتها إلا بعد النظر والتأمل، وإنما الكلام في ماهية النفس، ولا يجوز أن يكون هذا ذهولاً عن العلم بالعلم، لأنه أيضاً مما يلزم دوامه سيما وهم يقولون : إن علمنا بذاتنا نفس ذاتنا وثانيها: أن حصول الشيء للشيء وحضوره عنده يقتضي تغاير الشيئين ضرورة فيمتنع علم الشيء بنفسه. وثالثها: أن النفس إذا كانت عالمة بذاتها وصفاتها كانت عالمة بعلمها بذلك، وهلم جراً لا إلى نهاية فيلزم علوم غير متناهية بالفعل، وأجيب عن الأول بمنع مقدمات بطلان اللازم وهو مكابرة وعن الأخيرين بأن التغاير الاعتباري كاف والاعتبارات العقلية تنقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار. وحاصله أن ليس هناك إلا شيء واحد هو ذلك المجرد المدرك وهو ليس بغائب عن نفسه، فمن حيث يعتبر شاهداً يكون عالماً. ومن حيث يعتبر مشهوداً يكون معلوماً ، ومن حيث يعتبر مشهودا يكون علماً، ومرجعه إلى أن وجود الشيء عين حصوله، وحضوره لا يزيد عليه بحسب الخارج .
Its quiddity and reality are only after consideration and reflection, but the discussion is about the quiddity of the soul, and it is not allowed that this be forgetfulness of the knowledge of knowledge, because it also necessitates its permanence, especially as they say: our knowledge of our essence is our essence itself. Second: that a thing's attainment for a thing and its presence with it necessitates the distinction of the two things necessarily, so knowledge of a thing by itself is impossible. Third: that if the soul is knowing of its essence and attributes, it would be knowing of its knowledge of that, and so on ad infinitum, leading to actual infinite knowledges. And it was answered concerning the first by denying the premises of the falsity of the consequent, which is stubbornness. And concerning the last two by saying that conceptual distinction suffices, and mental considerations cease with the cessation of consideration. The conclusion is that there is nothing but one thing, which is that abstract perceiver, and it is not absent from itself. So insofar as it is considered as witness, it is knower; insofar as it is considered as witnessed, it is known; and insofar as it is considered as witnessed, it is knowledge. Its return is that the existence of a thing is identical to its attainment, and its presence does not add to it externally.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the soul's knowledge of itself and provides an answer. It begins by stating that the quiddity of the soul requires reflection. Then it lists three objections: (1) The soul's knowledge of itself would be its own essence, leading to circularity. (2) Knowledge requires a distinction between knower and known, so self-knowledge is impossible. (3) If the soul knows itself, it would know that it knows, leading to infinite regress. The answer to the first is dismissed as stubbornness (mukābara). To the last two, the answer is that a conceptual distinction (taghāyur iʿtibārī) suffices. The conclusion is that the soul is one abstract entity that, from different aspects, is knower, known, and knowledge. Existence and presence are identical; the presence of a thing to itself is not a separate addition.

Correction: The author argues that the soul is one reality; the distinction between knower and known is only conceptual (taghāyur iʿtibārī).

The Perceiver Does Not Acquire Characteristics of the Perceived
Author argues that the perceiver does not acquire qualities of the perceived due to form-essence distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the perceiver (the soul or the mind) does not take on the qualities of the things it perceives, based on the distinction between form and essence.

Core Idea
Perceiving something does not make you become it.

Have you ever wondered why thinking about heat doesn't make you hot, or imagining sadness doesn't actually make you sad? This lesson tackles that very question. Sanusi explains a foundational principle of Islamic epistemology: the perceiver and the perceived are separate realms. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between mental concepts and external realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms like الصورة and الهوية.
2
Identify the argument structure
Identify the main claim, the reason given (التغاير), the examples of sensible qualities, the additional justification about modes of existence, and the extension to intellectual qualities.
3
Reflect on the contrast
Notice the final contrast with physical bodies. Ask yourself: why does the body acquire blackness but the soul does not?
This reinforces the unique nature of mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدرك لا يتصف بما يدركه لتغاير الصورة والهوية. ولما بين صورة الشيء وهويته من التغاير لم يلزم اتصاف المدرك بما يدركه من السواد والحرارة والاستدارة ونحو ذلك على أن حصول الصورة للنفس ليس كحصول العرض للجوهر ولهذا لا يلزم من إدراك المعاني العقلية أيضاً كالإيمان والكفر والجود والبخل اتصاف النفس بها، وإنما الكلام في عكسه فكيف في مثل حصول السواد والبياض للجسم
The perceiver is not characterized by what it perceives due to the distinction between the form and the essence. And when there is a distinction between the form of a thing and its essence, it does not necessitate that the perceiver be characterized by what it perceives, such as blackness, heat, roundness and the like, especially since the attainment of the form by the soul is not like the inherence of an accident in a substance. For this reason, the perception of intellectual meanings such as faith, disbelief, generosity, and miserliness also does not necessitate the soul's being characterized by them. Rather, the discussion is about its opposite, so how about such as the attainment of blackness and whiteness in the body?
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage establishes a key epistemological principle: the perceiver (al-mudrik) does not take on the properties of the perceived. The reason is the distinction between the form (sūra) and the essence (huwiyya) of a thing. The form is a mental representation, not the actual essence. For example, when we perceive blackness or heat, the soul does not become black or hot. The author notes that the attainment of the form in the soul is not like the inherence of an accident in a substance; it is a different kind of existence. This also explains why we can conceive of abstract moral qualities like faith without becoming those qualities ourselves. The passage then contrasts this with physical bodies where the inherence of a quality does affect the substance.

Correction: The form and essence are distinct; perceiving a quality does not make the perceiver acquire that quality.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The perceiver does not become characterized by what it perceives because mental forms are distinct from external essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defending the Theory of Mental Forms: Attention and Perception
Author addresses objection that perception is of external objects, not mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the theory that perception involves mental forms against the objection that we perceive external objects directly.

Core Idea
Perception requires attention, not just presence of the form.

This passage addresses a natural objection: don't we directly see blackness itself, not its mental phantom? The author argues that this does not disprove the theory of mental forms. The key insight is that perception is not identical to the mere presence of a form; the soul's attention (iltifāt) is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objection: it seems we perceive external objects directly (blackness, human) rather than mental images. Understand why this might challenge the theory of mental forms.
The objection is based on common sense experience.
2
Follow the author's response
The author says this objection 'does not harm' (لا يقدح). Perception is attributed to the soul or common sense, while the form's attainment occurs in a bodily substrate.
The author distinguishes between the location of the form and the act of perception.
3
Notice the role of attention
The author points out that attainment can occur without perception if the soul is not paying attention. This shows that perception requires an additional factor: attention (iltifāt).
This is the key defense: perception is not automatic upon form presence.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider how this argument preserves the theory of mental forms while explaining direct realism. The forms are in the senses or common sense, but perception occurs only when the soul attends to them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يقدح في ذلك أن المبصر هو هذا السواد لا شبحه والمتعقل هو الإنسان لا صورته وإنكم تجعلون الإدراك للنفس، أو للحس المشترك مع أن الحصول في الجليدية مثلاً، وأنه ربما يتحقق الحصول فيها مع عدم الإدراك لعدم التفات النفس
Then it does not harm that the seen is this blackness, not its phantom, and the intelligible is the human being, not his form, and you attribute perception to the soul or to the common sense, while the attainment is in the [؟] for example, and that sometimes attainment occurs there without perception due to the lack of attention of the soul.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: it seems that we perceive external objects directly (e.g., we see this blackness, not an image of it) and we understand the human, not just his mental form. The author says this does not harm the earlier principle. The perception is attributed to the soul or to the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), while the attainment (husūl) of the form occurs in a substrate (perhaps 'al-jalidiyya' – the cortical or bodily organ?). Sometimes attainment occurs without perception because the soul is not paying attention. This shows that perception is not identical to mere presence of the form; attention (iltifāt) is needed.

Correction: The author holds that we can say we perceive the external object (e.g., this blackness) as a conventional expression, but the perception is mediated by a form that is present in the sense or common sense. The objection misses the nuance that perception requires attention to that form.

Mental Forms: Accidents in the Mind, Substances in the World
Author explains the ontological status of mental forms as accidents in the mind but representing substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mental forms are accidents in the intellect while representing external substances, and how they are both particular and universal.

Core Idea
Mental forms are accidents in the mind but mirror external substances.

This lesson begins a deep exploration of the ontological status of mental forms. The author clarifies a crucial point: although mental forms are accidents in the intellect, this does not contradict that the quiddities they represent are substances in external reality. This lays the foundation for understanding how knowledge relates to its objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a compressed style.
2
Identify the key claim about mental forms
The passage states that mental forms are accidents in the mind. Find this and note how the author immediately qualifies it.
The qualification is that this does not negate the quiddity being a substance externally.
3
Understand particularity and universality
The author says the form is particular due to its subsistence in a particular soul, and universal in terms of its relation to individuals. Ask yourself how these two attributes can coexist.
This is a key point in kalam epistemology.
4
Reflect on the analogy with existence
The relation of occurrence to the form is compared to existence to quiddity externally. Consider what this implies about the ontological addition of the intellect.
The author says there is no addition except by consideration of the intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الصور العلمية وإن كانت من حيث حصولها في العقل عرضاً لم تناف كون الماهية المعقولة جوهراً بمعنى أنها إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، كما أنها تكون جزئياً لقيامها بالنفس الجزئية، وكلياً من حيث انشائها إلى الأفراد ونسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهيه في الخارج فلا زيادة إلا باعتبار العقل، ومن قد يجعل العلم نفس الصورة فهي من حيث إن الحصول نفسها علم وعرض موجود في الأعيان كسائر صفات النفس، ومن حيث إنه زائد عليها مفهوم ولا تحقق له إلا في الأذهان، وأما المعلوم فهو مساله الصورة لا نفسها، إلا أن يستأنف لها تعقل ويلحقها أحكام هي المعقولات الثانية، وبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل الكلية من عوارض المعلوم حقيقة
Then the scientific forms, even though in terms of their occurrence in the intellect they are accidents, do not contradict that the intelligible quiddity is a substance – meaning that if it existed externally, it would not be in a subject. Also, they are particular due to their subsistence in the particular soul, and universal in terms of their [؟] to individuals. The relation of occurrence to the form in the intellect is the relation of existence to quiddity in the external, so there is no addition except by the consideration of the intellect. And some may make knowledge identical to the form, so it is, in terms of that the occurrence itself is knowledge, an accident existing in concrete reality like other attributes of the soul; and in terms that it is additional to it, a notion that has no realization except in minds. As for the known (al-maʿlūm), it is the [؟] of the form, not the form itself, unless a new intellection is initiated for it and judgments attach to it which are the secondary intelligibles. And by this consideration, it is valid to posit universality as an attribute of the known in reality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage explains the ontological status of mental forms (al-ṣuwar al-ʿilmiyya). They are accidents (aʿrāḍ) insofar as they occur in the intellect, but this does not conflict with the fact that the quiddity (māhiyya) they represent is a substance externally. They are particular because they inhere in a particular soul, yet universal because they apply to many individuals (via a process of abstraction? 'inshā'ihā' might mean 'their derivation'). The relation of the form's occurrence to the intellect is analogous to the relation of existence to quiddity externally; there is no extra entity except conceptual addition. The author also notes that some thinkers identify knowledge with the form itself. Then he distinguishes between the mental form as a cognitive act and as a content. The known (al-maʿlūm) is the 'example' (mithāl?) of the form, not the form itself. When we apply secondary intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) like universality, those are attributes of the known, not the form as a mental entity.

Key Terms
الصور العلمية
العقل
الماهية
الجوهر
العرض
الكلية
الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the mental form with the external essence and thinking universals exist concretely as independent entities.
Correction: The mental form is an accident in the mind and represents the external substance; universality is a conceptual attribution, not a concrete existent.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the relationship between mental forms and the external quiddity?
A) They are identical
B) The form is an accident in the mind while the quiddity is a substance externally
C) The form is a substance and the quiddity is an accident
D) Both are accidents

Key Takeaway
Mental forms are accidents in the intellect but do not negate the substantiality of external quiddities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You carry countless mental forms every day—ideas of God, truth, justice, and your own self. This lesson reveals that each of these forms is an accident in your soul, not the reality itself. Are you guilty of treating your mental image of God as if it were God Himself? What would it mean for your worship to distinguish between the form in your mind and the Reality it represents?

?

Yet how often do you act as if your knowledge is just a subjective illusion? What would change if you truly believed that your mental forms—when correct—mirror real substances?

Distinguishing Form from Known and the Role of Secondary Intelligibles
Author distinguishes the mental form from the known and explains secondary intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the mental form as an act of knowledge and the known (ma'lum), and how universality is an attribute of the known through secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
The known is not the form itself but its content; universality belongs to the known.

In this continuation, the author delves deeper into the nature of knowledge and its object. He discusses an alternative view that identifies knowledge with the form itself, then clarifies that the known (al-maʿlūm) is the example of the form, not the form itself. He explains how secondary intelligibles attach to the known, making universality a genuine attribute of the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two aspects of the form discussed.
Pay attention to the shift in perspective.
2
Identify the two aspects of the form
The author presents a view that makes knowledge identical to the form. In one aspect, it is an accident in concrete reality; in another, it is a notion only in minds.
This dual aspect is important for understanding the nature of knowledge.
3
Grasp the definition of the known
The known (ma'lum) is described as the 'example' of the form, not the form itself. Understand this distinction.
The known is the content or intelligible object, not the mental act.
4
Understand the role of secondary intelligibles
Secondary intelligibles like universality are judgments attached to the known through a new act of intellection.
This makes universality a real attribute of the known, not of the form as a mental entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الصور العلمية وإن كانت من حيث حصولها في العقل عرضاً لم تناف كون الماهية المعقولة جوهراً بمعنى أنها إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، كما أنها تكون جزئياً لقيامها بالنفس الجزئية، وكلياً من حيث انشائها إلى الأفراد ونسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهيه في الخارج فلا زيادة إلا باعتبار العقل، ومن قد يجعل العلم نفس الصورة فهي من حيث إن الحصول نفسها علم وعرض موجود في الأعيان كسائر صفات النفس، ومن حيث إنه زائد عليها مفهوم ولا تحقق له إلا في الأذهان، وأما المعلوم فهو مساله الصورة لا نفسها، إلا أن يستأنف لها تعقل ويلحقها أحكام هي المعقولات الثانية، وبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل الكلية من عوارض المعلوم حقيقة
Then the scientific forms, even though in terms of their occurrence in the intellect they are accidents, do not contradict that the intelligible quiddity is a substance – meaning that if it existed externally, it would not be in a subject. Also, they are particular due to their subsistence in the particular soul, and universal in terms of their [؟] to individuals. The relation of occurrence to the form in the intellect is the relation of existence to quiddity in the external, so there is no addition except by the consideration of the intellect. And some may make knowledge identical to the form, so it is, in terms of that the occurrence itself is knowledge, an accident existing in concrete reality like other attributes of the soul; and in terms that it is additional to it, a notion that has no realization except in minds. As for the known (al-maʿlūm), it is the [؟] of the form, not the form itself, unless a new intellection is initiated for it and judgments attach to it which are the secondary intelligibles. And by this consideration, it is valid to posit universality as an attribute of the known in reality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage explains the ontological status of mental forms (al-ṣuwar al-ʿilmiyya). They are accidents (aʿrāḍ) insofar as they occur in the intellect, but this does not conflict with the fact that the quiddity (māhiyya) they represent is a substance externally. They are particular because they inhere in a particular soul, yet universal because they apply to many individuals (via a process of abstraction? 'inshā'ihā' might mean 'their derivation'). The relation of the form's occurrence to the intellect is analogous to the relation of existence to quiddity externally; there is no extra entity except conceptual addition. The author also notes that some thinkers identify knowledge with the form itself. Then he distinguishes between the mental form as a cognitive act and as a content. The known (al-maʿlūm) is the 'example' (mithāl?) of the form, not the form itself. When we apply secondary intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) like universality, those are attributes of the known, not the form as a mental entity.

الصورة
المعلوم

Checkpoint

B) The known is the example of the form
C) The known is the form itself
D) The known is a secondary intelligible

Key Takeaway
The known is the content of the form, not the form itself; universality is a secondary intelligible attributed to the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says the known is the content of the form, not the form itself. How often do you mistake your mental images for the actual realities they represent? In what area of your life might this confusion be causing you to miss the true object of knowledge?

Refuting the Objection That Knowing Heat Makes the Soul Hot
Sanusi refutes the objection that knowing heat entails the soul becoming hot.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the essence (huwiyya) and the form (sura) and how it resolves the objection that knowing heat would make the soul hot.

Core Idea
The mental form of heat does not impart heat; only the external essence does.

In this passage, the author tackles a classic objection against the theory of knowledge as the attainment of the form. If knowing heat required the soul to become hot, that would lead to absurdities like the soul simultaneously being hot and cold. The answer lies in a precise distinction between the essence of a thing and its form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that if knowledge is the form of a thing, then knowing heat would make the knower hot and round, and even combine opposites.
This objection was raised by al-Imam Fakhr al-Razi.
2
Grasp the answer
The author distinguishes between the essence (huwiyya) which is particular and causally effective, and the form (sura) which is universal and abstract.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how this distinction saves the theory of knowledge as form from absurdity.
The form is equivalent to the essence in quiddity but not in its effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى دفع اعتراضات للإمام وغيره منها: أن العلم لو كان حصول الصورة المساوية التي ربما تسمى ماهية الشيء لزم من تصور الحرارة والاستدارة كون القوة المدركة حارة مستديرة وكذا جميع الكيفيات، وهو مع ظهور فساده يستلزم اجتماع الضدين كالحرارة والبرودة عند تصورهما. وجوابه: أن الحار ما قام به هوية الحرارة لا صورته، وماهيته وكذا جميع الصفات، وفرق ما بينهما ظاهر فإن الهوية جزئية محفوفة بالعوارض فاعلة للصفات الخارجية والصورة كلية مجردة لا تلحقها الأحكام، ولا يترتب عليها الآثار. وهذا لا ينافي مساواتها للهوية، بمعنى أنها بحيث إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت إياها
A reference to repulsing objections of al-Imam and others, among them: that if knowledge were the attainment of the equivalent form which may be called the quiddity of a thing, then it would follow from conceiving heat and roundness that the perceiving power is hot and round, and likewise all qualities. And that, while its unsoundness is apparent, entails the combination of opposites like heat and cold when imagining them. Its answer: that the hot is that to which the essence (huwiyya) of heat subsists, not its form and quiddity, and likewise all qualities. And the difference between them is clear: the essence is particular, surrounded by accidents, effective for external qualities, while the form is universal, abstract, not subject to judgments, and does not entail effects. And this does not contradict its equivalence to the essence, meaning that if it existed externally it would be it.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific objection attributed to al-Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) and others: if knowledge is the attainment of the form that is equivalent to the quiddity, then knowing heat would make the perceiving faculty hot, and knowing cold would make it cold, leading to impossible contradictions. The answer distinguishes clearly: heat (the property) belongs to that which has the essence (huwiyya) of heat inherent in it, not to the form. The essence is particular, accident-laden, and causally effective. The form is universal, abstract, and does not produce physical effects. This distinction does not negate the equivalence (musāwāt) between form and essence, meaning that the form, if instantiated externally, would be identical to the essence.

الماهية

المعقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mental form of a quality like heat possesses the same causal properties as the external quality, so knowing heat would make the soul hot.
Correction: The form is abstract and universal; only the particular essence (huwiyya) has causal effects. The form is equivalent to the essence only in terms of quiddity, not in its external effects.

Checkpoint

B) The essence is particular and causally effective, while the form is universal and abstract
C) The essence is universal and the form is particular
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
The key distinction between essence (huwiyya) and form (sura) resolves the objection that knowing heat would make the soul hot, preserving the theory of knowledge as the attainment of the form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a quality you strongly associate with a person or situation – like fear, anger, or love. Is your mental image of that quality causing real effects in your heart? How can you distinguish between the form in your mind and its actual essence, as this lesson teaches?
?
When you learn about Allah's attributes (e.g., His power, mercy), do you sometimes imagine them as if they were physical qualities? How does the distinction between form (abstract concept) and essence (particular reality) protect you from reducing Allah to a mental image?

Two Types of Husul: Attributive and Perceptual
Defines two types of husul and explains perceptual obtaining does not entail description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributive and perceptual obtaining and why perceptual obtaining does not entail description of the soul.

Core Idea
Perceptual obtaining is presence without description; only attributive obtaining describes.

This passage introduces a crucial distinction in Islamic epistemology: the difference between when a quality actually inheres in a subject (attributive obtaining) and when it is merely present to awareness (perceptual obtaining). The author argues that the soul can perceive its own states without being described by them, because the two types of obtaining operate differently. This distinction is essential for understanding how we know God and His attributes without the soul becoming divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the various examples of 'husul' the author lists.
2
Identify the two types of husul
Find where the author distinguishes between attributive obtaining (husul al-'ard fi mahallihi) and perceptual obtaining (husul al-hadir 'inda al-hadir). Understand the difference.
Attributive obtaining entails description; perceptual obtaining does not.
3
Understand the argument about the soul
The author applies this distinction to the soul's perception of its own attributes (faith, generosity). See how he concludes that perception alone doesn't make the soul described by those attributes.
This is the key application of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواب آخر، وهو أن حصول الشيء للشيء يقال لمعان متعددة كحصول المال لصاحبه وبالعكس وحصول السواد للجسم وبالعكس، وحصول السرعة للحركة، وحصول الصورة للمادة وبالعكس وحصول كل منهما للجسم وبالعكس، وحصول الحاضر لما حضر عنده وبالعكس ولزوم الاتصاف إنما هو في حصول العرض بمحله ولا كذلك حصول الحاضر لما حضر عنده وبالعكس وهو معلوم لنا بالوجدان ومتحقق كونه حصولاً لنا وإن لم نقدر على التعبير عن خصوصيته بغير كونه إدراكاً وعلماً أو شعوراً أو إحاطة بكنه الشيء، أو ما يجري مجرى هذه الاعتبارات وبهذا أعني لكون الحصول الإدراكي مغايراً لحصول العرض للمحل المستلزم للاتصاف لا يلزم من إدراك المعاني التي تكون من صفات النفس كالإيمان والكفر، والجود والبخل ونحو ذلك اتصاف النفس بها لانتفاء الحصول الاتصافي، فكيف يلزم ذلك فيما ليس من شأن النفس الاتصاف بها كالحرارة والاستداره ونحو ذلك
Another answer: that the obtaining of a thing for a thing is said with multiple meanings—like the obtaining of wealth for its owner and vice versa, the obtaining of blackness for the body and vice versa, the obtaining of speed for motion, the obtaining of form for matter and vice versa, the obtaining of each of them for the body and vice versa, and the obtaining of the present for that which is present to it and vice versa. The entailment of description is only in the obtaining of an accident in its subject, and not so the obtaining of the present for that which is present to it and vice versa. And it is known to us by intuition, and it is realized as an obtaining for us even if we are unable to express its specificity except by its being perception, knowledge, feeling, or encompassing the essence of the thing, or what runs in the course of these considerations. And by this—I mean because perceptual obtaining is different from the obtaining of an accident in the subject that entails description—it does not follow from perceiving meanings that are attributes of the soul, like faith and disbelief, generosity and stinginess, and the like, that the soul is described by them, due to the absence of attributive obtaining. So how could that follow in what is not apt for the soul to be described by, like heat, roundness, and the like?
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of 'husul' (obtaining) and clarifies their implications. The author begins by listing various senses of 'husul'—such as ownership, inherence, motion, form-matter, and presence. He then distinguishes between attributive obtaining (husul al-'ard fi mahallihi), which entails description (ittisaf) of the subject by the accident, and perceptual obtaining (husul al-hadir 'inda al-hadir), which is mere presence without entailing description. He emphasizes that perceptual obtaining is known intuitively and is essentially the same as perception, knowledge, or awareness. Based on this distinction, he argues that when the soul perceives its own attributes (e.g., faith), it does not become described by them because perceptual obtaining lacks the entailment of description. This is even more evident for external qualities like heat and roundness, which are not even attributes of the soul. The key point is that the two types of obtaining are fundamentally different operations.

الحصول الإدراكي
الحصول الاتصافي
الاتصاف
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any obtaining (husul) of a form in the soul entails that the soul is described by that form.
Correction: The author clarifies that only attributive obtaining (inherence of an accident in a subject) entails description; perceptual obtaining is mere presence without description.

Checkpoint

B) Because perceptual obtaining does not entail description.
C) Because the soul cannot perceive mental attributes.
D) Because the soul is already generous by nature.

Key Takeaway
Perceptual obtaining is a mode of presence distinct from inherence; therefore, perceiving an attribute does not make the perceiver described by it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Question: Does Attributive Obtaining Entail Perceptual Obtaining?
Introduces the question and the author's core distinction between form and identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributive obtaining and perceptual obtaining, and why the author argues that they are not identical.

Core Idea
Attributive obtaining does not automatically give perceptual awareness.

This lesson tackles a subtle philosophical question: if something has an attribute, must it also be aware of that attribute? The author begins by framing the debate and then offers a crucial distinction between two types of 'obtaining' that will be used to counter a well-known objection. Pay close attention to the concepts of form (surah) and identity (huwiyyah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the English translation. Try to get the overall argument before diving into details.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author states that the opponents did not clarify whether they claim mere attributive obtaining is sufficient for perception or that it entails perceptual obtaining. Spot these two options in the text.
This sets up the problem: the opponents have not defined their position precisely.
3
Understand the author's own position
The author declares that 'the truth is that all obtainings are with a non-originating existence which is the form.' Consider what this means: the form is a separate entity that allows us to talk about obtaining without implying perception.
This is the key move: distinguishing the form from the subject's identity.
4
Refute the objection about distinction
The author then addresses a counter: if the form is distinct from the possessor, then there would be two similars (the form and the attribute) which should not coexist. He replies that the similarity is between identities, not forms. Grasp this subtle point.
This defends his position from a logical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما الكلام في أن الحصول الاتصافي هل يستلزم الحصول الإدراكي حتى يلزم دوام تعقل النفس لصفاتها على ما زعموا ثم إنهم لم يبينوا أن ذلك مبنى على أن مجرد الحصول الاتصافي كاف في الإدراك النفسي لصفاتها، أو على أنه مستلزم للحصول الإدراكي والحق أن الكل بوجود غير متأصل هو الصورة، ذلك التمايز بين الصورة وذي الصورة ولصفاتها اجتماع المثلين مدفوع بما مر من التغاير بين الصورة والهوية وبأن التماثل المانع من الاجتماع، إنما هو بين المهويتين. ولو سلم فبطريق الحصول الاتصافي، وبالجملة إذا كان الحصول الإدراكي غير الحصول الاتصافي، ولم يتحقق كون الحصول الاتصافي لما من شأنه الإدراك مستلزماً للإدراك كان عدم استلزامه فيما ليس من شأنه الإدراك، كحصول السواد للحجر أولى، فلا يرد ما ذكر الإمام من أن الإدراك إذا كان نفس الحصول كان المدرك هو الذي ليس له الحصول وكان الجسم الحار مدركاً للحرارة
The discussion is only about whether attributive obtaining entails perceptual obtaining, such that the soul would necessarily always be aware of its attributes as they claimed. Then they did not clarify whether that is based on mere attributive obtaining being sufficient for the soul's perception of its attributes, or on it entailing perceptual obtaining. And the truth is that all [obtainings] are with a non-originating existence which is the form. That distinction between the form and the possessor of form and the conjunction of two similars for its attributes is refuted by what was mentioned earlier of the difference between form and identity, and that the similarity that prevents conjunction is only between the two identities. And if it is granted, then it is by way of attributive obtaining. In sum: if perceptual obtaining is different from attributive obtaining, and it has not been established that attributive obtaining, for what is capable of perception, entails perception, then its non-entailment for what is not capable of perception, like the obtaining of blackness in a stone, is more appropriate. So what the Imam mentioned does not apply: that if perception is identical to obtaining, then the perceiver would be that which has the obtaining, and the hot body would be perceiving heat.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by focusing on a key question: does attributive obtaining (husul ittisafi) necessarily imply perceptual obtaining (husul idraki)? The author notes that the opponents did not clarify whether they claim that mere attributive obtaining is sufficient for perception or that it entails perceptual obtaining. He then states that the truth is that all obtainings are with a non-fundamental existence, which is the form. He refutes an objection about the soul perceiving itself leading to a lack of distinction between form and possessor of form, arguing that the distinction is maintained by the difference between form (surah) and identity (huwiyyah). If granted, the perception would be through attributive obtaining, not perceptual. The conclusion is that since perceptual and attributive obtaining are distinct, and attributive obtaining does not necessarily entail perception (especially for things not capable of perception), the objection of Imam al-Ghazali (that if perception were identical to obtaining, a hot body would perceive its heat) is invalid. The key point is that the two types of obtaining remain distinct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having an attribute necessarily means being aware of that attribute.
Correction: Attributive obtaining (having an attribute) is distinct from perceptual obtaining (awareness), so a subject can have an attribute without perceiving it.

Checkpoint

A) They are all perceptual in nature.

C) They are all identical to the subject's identity.

Key Takeaway
Attributive obtaining and perceptual obtaining are distinct, and the form serves as a mediating entity that prevents conflation.

?

Refuting the Imam's Objection on Perception and Obtaining
Concludes that perceptual obtaining is distinct and refutes the Imam's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes Imam al-Ghazali's argument that if perception were identical to obtaining, a hot body would perceive its heat.

Core Idea
Perceptual obtaining is different from attributive obtaining, so the Imam's objection fails.

In this lesson, we see the author deliver the final blow to the opponent's argument. He builds on the previous distinction and shows that even if we grant attributive obtaining as a basis for perception, it would not make a hot body aware of its heat. The key is that perceptual obtaining is a distinct type that only applies to perceivers.

Guided Path
1
Read the remaining passage
Start from 'ولو سلم' and read through to the end. This part contains the main logical argument and the refutation of the Imam's specific objection.
2
Analyze the conditional 'ولو سلم'
The author says 'And if it is granted, then it is by way of attributive obtaining.' Understand what exactly is being granted here: the possibility of similarity leading to conjunction? Or more likely, the possibility of perception through attributive obtaining. See how he turns the opponent's own tools against them.
This step shows the author's dialectical skill: he concedes a point but limits its scope.
3
Understand the main argument (البالجملة)
The author summarizes: perceptual obtaining is different from attributive obtaining. Therefore, even if attributive obtaining were to entail perception in things that are capable of perception, it does not follow that it does so in things not capable of perception, like a stone or a hot body. This is the core of the refutation.
4
See how the Imam's objection is avoided
The Imam had argued: if perception is just obtaining, then a hot body obtains heat, so it would perceive heat. The author replies that the hot body's obtaining is attributive, not perceptual, so the objection does not apply.

Source Text

The discussion is only about whether attributive obtaining entails perceptual obtaining, such that the soul would necessarily always be aware of its attributes as they claimed. And the truth is that all [obtainings] are with a non-originating existence which is the form. That distinction between the form and the possessor of form and the conjunction of two similars for its attributes is refuted by what was mentioned earlier of the difference between form and identity, and that the similarity that prevents conjunction is only between the two identities. And if it is granted, then it is by way of attributive obtaining. In sum: if perceptual obtaining is different from attributive obtaining, and it has not been established that attributive obtaining, for what is capable of perception, entails perception, then its non-entailment for what is not capable of perception, like the obtaining of blackness in a stone, is more appropriate. So what the Imam mentioned does not apply: that if perception is identical to obtaining, then the perceiver would be that which has the obtaining, and the hot body would be perceiving heat.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by focusing on a key question: does attributive obtaining (husul ittisafi) necessarily imply perceptual obtaining (husul idraki)? The author notes that the opponents did not clarify whether they claim that mere attributive obtaining is sufficient for perception or that it entails perceptual obtaining. He then states that the truth is that all obtainings are with a non-fundamental existence, which is the form. He refutes an objection about the soul perceiving itself leading to a lack of distinction between form and possessor of form, arguing that the distinction is maintained by the difference between form (surah) and identity (huwiyyah). If granted, the perception would be through attributive obtaining, not perceptual. The conclusion is that since perceptual and attributive obtaining are distinct, and attributive obtaining does not necessarily entail perception (especially for things not capable of perception), the objection of Imam al-Ghazali (that if perception were identical to obtaining, a hot body would perceive its heat) is invalid. The key point is that the two types of obtaining remain distinct.

Key Terms

الحصول الاتصافي
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception is identical to having an attribute (obtaining).
Correction: Perception (perceptual obtaining) is a distinct type of obtaining that only applies to beings capable of awareness. Simply having an attribute does not imply awareness of it.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion regarding the hot body example?
A) The hot body would indeed perceive its heat.
B) The hot body's obtaining of heat is attributive, not perceptual, so it does not perceive it.
C) The hot body cannot have heat because it is not a perceiver.
D) The Imam's objection is accepted but reinterpreted.

Key Takeaway
Perceptual obtaining is a special kind of obtaining that only occurs in beings capable of perception; mere attributive obtaining does not constitute awareness.

?

?

Objections on Perception and Their Refutation
Presents and refutes two objections on the nature of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key objections against the view that perception is the obtaining of a form at the perceiver, and how the author refutes them by clarifying the locus of perception.

Core Idea
Perception is the obtaining of a form at the perceiver, not in the sense instrument.

This passage presents two powerful objections against the idea that perception involves obtaining a form. The first objection claims that we directly perceive external objects, not mere forms — otherwise we'd be endorsing sophistry. The second objection turns on the location of the form: if perception is the obtaining of a form, and the form obtains in the eye or imagination, then those instruments would be the perceivers. The author's careful answers clarify his doctrine and show how it avoids these pitfalls, while also repelling a third objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the polite but firm tone of the objections and answers.
The passage is dense, so read twice if needed.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection says we know the perceived is the external object itself; calling it an image is sophistry. The second objection says if perception is obtaining, then the instrument (e.g., eye) would be the perceiver, since the form obtains there.
The third objection is introduced but cut off; it is repelled by the second answer.
3
Understand the author's answers
For the first, the author agrees the perceived is the object, but explains that perception is the obtaining of a form from it at the perceiver. For the second, he clarifies that perception is the obtaining at the perceiver (soul) of the obtaining in the instrument, so the instrument is not the perceiver.
These answers preserve the distinction between the locus of the form's instantiation and the subject of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنا نعلم قطعاً أن المدرك بالحس أو العقل هو الموجود العيني كهذا السواد وهذا الصوت والإنسان فالقول بأنه مثال وشبح من الموجود لا نفسه يكون سفسطة، والجواب: أنه لا نزاع في أن المدرك هو ذلك الموجود، لكن إدراكه عبارة عن حصول صورة منه ومثال عند المدرك بحصولها فيه، أو في آلته ومنها أنكم تجعلون المدرك للمحسوسات هو النفس، أو الحس المشترك مع أن حصول الصورة ليس فيها، بل في الخيال، أو غيره من الآلات كالرطوبة الجليدية للمبصرات، فلو كان الإدراك هو الحصول لكان المدرك ما فيه الحصول والجواب: أنا لا نجعل إدراك المحسوسات هو الحصول في الآلة بل الحصول عند المدرك للحصول في الآلة فلا يلزم ما ذكر وبهذا يندفع اعتراض آخر وهو أنه لو كان مجرد الحضور عند الحس على ما هو المراد بالمشاهدة كافياً في الإدراك لكان
And among them: that we know for certain that the perceived by sense or intellect is the external existent like this blackness, this sound, and a human; so the statement that it is an image and apparition of the existent, not itself, is sophistry. The answer: there is no dispute that the perceived is that existent, but its perception consists in the obtaining of a form from it and an image at the perceiver by its obtaining in it or in its instrument. And among them: that you make the perceiver of sensible objects the soul or the common sense, while the obtaining of the form is not in them, but in the imagination or other instruments like the vitreous humor for visible objects; so if perception were the obtaining, then the perceiver would be that in which the obtaining occurs. The answer: we do not make perception of sensible objects the obtaining in the instrument, but rather the obtaining at the perceiver of the obtaining in the instrument, so what you mentioned does not follow. And by this another objection is repelled: that if mere presence at the sense, as is meant by witnessing, were sufficient for perception, then...
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the author's position and answers each. The first objection: we know the perceived is the external object itself, not a form; calling it an image is sophistry. Answer: we agree the perceived is the object, but perception is the obtaining of a form from it; there is no contradiction. The second objection: you claim the soul or common sense perceives, but the form obtains in the imagination or other instruments (like the eye's vitreous humor), so if perception were obtaining, the instrument would be the perceiver. Answer: perception is not the obtaining in the instrument; rather, it is the obtaining at the perceiver (soul/common sense) of that obtaining in the instrument. The third objection (cut off): if mere presence at the sense ('presence' meaning 'witnessing') were sufficient, then... The answer is implied to be that it is not sufficient; the perceptual obtaining must occur in the perceiver proper. Each answer preserves the distinction between the locus of the form's instantiation and the subject of perception.

Refuting al-Razi: Cognitive vs Attributive Acquisition
Al-Taftazani refutes al-Razi's objection by distinguishing cognitive from attributive acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes al-Razi's objection by distinguishing cognitive acquisition from attributive acquisition.

Core Idea
Perception is cognitive acquisition, not attributive acquisition.

Al-Taftazani responds to a critical objection from Imam al-Razi, who argued that identifying perception with acquisition would lead to absurd consequences like a hot body perceiving its own heat. To resolve this, al-Taftazani introduces a crucial distinction between two types of acquisition: the cognitive presence of a form in the mind and the mere inherence of an attribute in a subject. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection attributed to al-Razi: if perception is acquisition, then a hot body acquiring heat would perceive heat.
The objection targets the identity thesis between perception and acquisition.
2
Understand the distinction
Al-Taftazani distinguishes cognitive acquisition (mental presence) from attributive acquisition (inherence of an attribute). This is the key move.
Cognitive acquisition occurs only in beings with perception; attributive acquisition occurs in any subject.
3
Trace the refutation
See how al-Taftazani argues that even if similarity between form and object is conceded, the similarity that prevents conjunction is between identities, not between form and possessor. Then he uses the distinction to block the absurd conclusion.
The argument relies on the idea that attributive acquisition does not entail perception, even for beings capable of perception.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction is important for the theory of perception. It preserves the identity of perception with cognitive acquisition while avoiding absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك التمايز بين الصورة وذي الصورة ولصفاتها اجتماع المثلين مدفوع بما مر من التغاير بين الصورة والهوية وبأن التماثل المانع من الاجتماع، إنما هو بين المهويتين. ولو سلم فبطريق الحصول الاتصافي، وبالجملة إذا كان الحصول الإدراكي غير الحصول الإتصافي، ولم يتحقق كون الحصول الاتصافي لما من شأنه الإدراك مستلزماً للإدراك كان عدم استلزامه فيما ليس من شأنه الإدراك، كحصول السواد للحجر أولى ، فلا يرد ما ذكر الإمام من أن الإدراك إذا كان نفس الحصول كان المدرك هو الذي ليس له الحصول وكان الجسم الحار مدركاً للحرارة.
That distinction between the form and the possessor of the form and its attributes [being] a conjunction of two similars is refuted by what has passed of the difference between the form and the identity, and because the similarity that prevents conjunction is only between the two identities. And if it is conceded, then [the implication follows] by way of attributive acquisition. In sum, if cognitive acquisition is different from attributive acquisition, and it is not established that attributive acquisition for something capable of perception entails perception, then its non-entailment for something not capable of perception, like the acquisition of blackness by a stone, is more fitting. So what the Imam (al-Razi) mentioned does not follow: that if perception were the same as acquisition, then the perceived would be that which does not have acquisition, and the hot body would perceive heat.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents al-Taftazani's reply to an objection attributed to Imam al-Razi. The objection argued that if perception is identified with the mere acquisition (ḥuṣūl) of a form, then a hot body that acquires heat would thereby perceive its own heat—an absurd conclusion. To refute this, al-Taftazani first clarifies a distinction between two types of acquisition: cognitive acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl al-idrākī) and attributive acquisition (al-ḥuṣūl al-ittiṣāfī). The former is the mental presence of the form in the perceiver, which constitutes perception; the latter is simply the inherence of an attribute in a subject, like blackness in a stone, which does not entail perception. He then argues that even if one concedes a similarity between the mental form and the external object, the similarity that would prevent conjunction (i.e., the existence of two identical forms in the same substrate) is between the two identities (al-māhīyāt), not between the form and its possessor. The key point is that cognitive acquisition is distinct from attributive acquisition, and there is no evidence that attributive acquisition in a being capable of perception necessarily produces perception. Therefore, it is even more obviously not productive of perception in a being not capable of it, such as a stone acquiring blackness. Thus, al-Razi's objection fails: a hot body does not perceive heat because its acquisition of heat is attributive, not cognitive.

Key Terms
صورة
حصول
إدراك
تماثل
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Taftazani denies any role for acquisition in perception.
Correction: He distinguishes cognitive acquisition, which constitutes perception, from attributive acquisition, which does not.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani assert that a hot body does not perceive its own heat?
A) Because heat is not a form
B) Because the hot body lacks the capacity for cognitive acquisition
C) Because heat is an accident and only substances can perceive
D) Because perception is the acquisition of the form in the soul, not attributive acquisition

Key Takeaway
The key to al-Taftazani's refutation is the distinction between cognitive and attributive acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Taftazani shows that perception is an active mental presence, not passive reception. In what areas of your life have you been passively absorbing influences rather than actively perceiving with your mind and heart? What will you do today to shift from attributive to cognitive engagement with the truth?

Response to Objections: Perception as Acquisition of Form in the Perceiver
Al-Taftazani defends his view against two objections about the locus of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends his view that perception is the acquisition of a form in the perceiver, not in the sense organ, by addressing two key objections.

Core Idea
Perception is representation, not direct contact; the form is in the instrument but present to the perceiver.

Al-Taftazani’s account of perception faces two seemingly powerful objections: first, that we directly perceive external existents, not mere images; second, that the form is located in the sense organ, not in the perceiver. He responds by carefully distinguishing between the existence of the form and its presence to the perceiver. This lesson walks through his reasoning and shows how it also blocks a third related objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with both objections
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow: two objections followed by responses, plus a third objection repelled.
Notice the pattern: objection → answer, then another objection → answer, then a final remark.
2
Understand the first objection and response
The first objection claims that direct perception of external existents makes any 'image' theory sophistry. Al-Taftazani agrees we perceive the existent but explains perception as acquisition of a form.
He doesn’t deny direct perception; he explains its mechanism.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The second objection points out that the form is in the imagination or eye, not in the soul/common sense. Al-Taftazani clarifies that perception is not the form’s existence in the instrument, but its presence at the perceiver via the instrument.
This distinction is key.
4
See how the answer repels a third implicit objection
The third objection was that mere presence to the sense (mushāhada) should suffice for perception, but an unattended object is not perceived. The reply: presence must include the form being present to the perceiver, not just the instrument.
This reinforces the need for the form’s presence at the perceiver.
5
Synthesize the view
Now you can state al-Taftazani’s definition: perception is the acquisition of a form at the perceiver (soul/common sense) by virtue of its existence in a sense instrument.
This avoids both sophistry and physical reductionism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنا نعلم قطعاً أن المدرك بالحس أو العقل هو الموجود العيني كهذا السواد وهذا الصوت والإنسان فالقول بأنه مثال وشبح من الموجود لا نفسه يكون سفسطة ، والجواب : أنه لا نزاع في أن المدرك هو ذلك الموجود، لكن إدراكه عبارة عن حصول صورة منه. ومثال عند المدرك بحصولها فيه، أو في آلته ومنها أنكم تجعلون المدرك للمحسوسات هو النفس، أو الحس المشترك مع أن حصول الصورة ليس فيها، بل في الخيال، أو غيره من الآلات كالرطوبة الجليدية للمبصرات، فلو كان الإدراك هو الحصول لكان المدرك ما فيه الحصول والجواب : أنا لا نجعل إدراك المحسوسات هو الحصول في الآلة بل الحصول عند المدرك للحصول في الآلة فلا يلزم ما ذكر وبهذا يندفع اعتراض آخر وهو أنه لو كان مجرد الحضور عند الحس على ما هو المراد بالمشاهدة كافياً في الإدراك لكان الحاضر الذي لا تلتفت إليه النفس ،مدركاً، وليس كذلك.
And among them [the objections] is that we know with certainty that the object perceived by sense or intellect is the external existent, like this blackness, this sound, and the human. So the statement that it is an example and phantom of the existent, not itself, is sophistry. The answer: There is no dispute that the perceived is that existent, but its perception is the acquisition of a form of it and an example at the perceiver by its acquisition in it or in its instrument. And among them is that you make the perceiver of sensible things the soul or the common sense, while the acquisition of the form is not in them, but in the imagination or other instruments like the vitreous humour for visible things. So if perception were the acquisition, then the perceiver would be that in which the acquisition occurs. The answer: We do not make the perception of sensibles the acquisition in the instrument, but rather the acquisition at the perceiver for the acquisition in the instrument, so what you mentioned does not follow. And by this another objection is repelled: that if mere presence to the sense, as meant by direct vision, were sufficient for perception, then something present to which the soul does not attend would be perceived, and it is not so.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage deals with two related objections and their joint answer. The first objection claims that we directly perceive external existents themselves, not mere images or phantoms. To say otherwise is sophistry. Al-Taftazani replies that he fully agrees we perceive the external existent, but explains that perception consists in the acquisition of a form of that existent in the perceiver. This form is an example (mithāl) that represents the object; perception is not the object itself but the representation. The second objection points out a problem of location: the soul or the common sense is said to be the perceiver, but the form is not acquired in them; it is in the imagination or in other physical instruments like the vitreous humour of the eye. Hence, if perception were simply the acquisition, the perceiver should be the instrument containing the form. Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between the locus of the form's existence and the locus of perception. The form exists in the instrument (e.g., the eye), but perception occurs when the form is present at the perceiver (the soul or common sense) by virtue of that acquisition in the instrument. Thus, the perceiver is not the instrument but the entity that has the form present to it through the instrument. This same answer also refutes a third implicit objection: if mere presence to the sense (mushāhada) were sufficient, then any object present even unnoticed would be perceived. But clearly, objects that the soul does not attend to are not perceived, so mere presence is insufficient; what matters is the form's presence at the perceiver.

Correction: He affirms we perceive the external existent, but explains that perception occurs through the acquisition of a form (image) of that object in the perceiver, not through direct contact.

Checkpoint

A) Because the imagination is not a sense organ
B) Because perception is the acquisition of the form at the perceiver, not the location of the form's existence
C) Because the imagination perceives only particulars
D) Because the form is not actually in the imagination

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends perception as the acquisition of a form of the object at the perceiver, rejecting that the locus of the form determines the perceiver.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you train yourself to perceive with full presence of the soul, especially in worship or contemplating Allah's signs?
?
Al-Taftazani teaches that the form of the object is not physically inside your soul, yet you perceive it. Does this remind you that your soul is not material? How does this affect your understanding of life after death, when the soul perceives without a physical body? Will you live today more conscious that your true identity is this perceiving soul?
?
The objection that mere presence to the sense would make everything 'perceived' is refuted by noting that the soul must attend. Are you missing spiritual realities that are present to your senses but ignored by your soul? What would it take for you to 'attend' to the signs of Allah that you encounter daily, so that they truly become perception? Commit to one specific practice this week to sharpen your soul's attention.

Answering Objections on Mental Forms: The Principle of Different Considerations
Al-Taftazani responds to three objections and provides a systematic defense using iʿtibār and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the correspondence between mental forms and external substances by distinguishing between different aspects of consideration and by drawing an analogy between mental acquisition and external existence.

Core Idea
A mental form can be both an accident and a substance — under different considerations, there is no contradiction.

Al-Taftazani here confronts several objections against the theory of knowledge as mental representation. Critics argue that an accident (the mental form) cannot correspond to a substance, nor be universal, and that al-Taftazani is inconsistent in defining knowledge. His answer masterfully employs the logical tool of iʿtibār (consideration) and an illuminating analogy. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
The passage begins with three objections. Identify each: (1) accident vs. substance, (2) particular vs. universal, (3) definitional inconsistency. Notice how they target apparent contradictions.
The objections are phrased as 'among them [the objections]' and 'also'.
2
Grasp the principle of different considerations
Al-Taftazani's core answer is that a single thing can have multiple descriptions under different aspects (iʿtibārāt). Focus on how he applies this to the accident/substance and particular/universal objections.
He distinguishes between existence in the mind and existence in the external world.
3
Understand the analogy with existence and quiddity
He compares the relation of acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the mental form with the relation of existence (wujūd) to quiddity (māhīyah). This clarifies the ontological status of mental forms.
The form in the intellect is the same quiddity that exists externally, but with an accidental attribute of 'being acquired'.
4
Reflect on the coherence of al-Taftazani's epistemology
Consider how this answer preserves both the intentionality of knowledge (correspondence) and its psychological reality (accident in the soul). How does this strengthen his critique of the skeptics?
This is a sophisticated solution that avoids both naive realism and skepticism.
