ومنها أن الصورة العلمية عرض قائم بالنفس وقد جعلتموها مطابقة للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون جوهراً، بل نفس ماهيته ، وامتناع كون العرض مطابقاً للجوهر، ونفس ماهيته معلوم بالضرورة وأيضاً : جعلتموها كلية مع أن كون العرض القائم بالنفس الجزئية جزئياً ضروري وأيضاً تجعلون العلم تارة حصول الصورة، وتارة نفس الصورة مع ظهور الفرق بينهما . والجواب: أن الممتنع هو كون الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد جوهراً ،وعرضاً، أو كلياً وجزئياً، وأما عند اختلاف الاعتبار فلا، فإن كون الصورة العقلية عرضاً من حيث كونها في الحال قائمة بالموضوع الذي هو النفس لا ينافي كونها جوهراً من حيث إنها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وإنما المستحيل كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً في الخارج بمعنى كونها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ولا في موضوع، وكذا كونها جزئية من حيث قيامها بالنفس الجزئية لا ينافي كليتها من حيث مطابقتها للأفراد الكثيرة، بمعنى أن الحاصل في العقل من كل منها عند التجريد عن العوارض تكون تلك الصورة بعينها، ثم نسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهية في الخارج، فكماله[؟] ليس للماهية تحقق في الخارج، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كذلك ليس للصورة تحقق في العقل، ولعارضها المسمى بالحصول تحقق آخر، بل الصورة في العقل هي نفس الماهية التي لها تحقق في الخارج، ولها عارض هو الحصول، ولو تقارنا في الصدق لما كان بينهما تقدم وتأخر.
And among them [the objections]: The scientific form is an accident subsisting in the soul, and you have made it corresponding to the external existent which may be a substance, indeed the very quiddity of it, and the impossibility of an accident corresponding to a substance, and to its very quiddity, is known necessarily. Also: You have made it universal while that an accident subsisting in a particular soul is necessarily particular. Also: You make knowledge sometimes the acquisition of the form, and sometimes the form itself, despite the apparent difference between them. The answer: What is impossible is that one thing under one consideration be both substance and accident, or universal and particular. But under different considerations it is not. For the mental form being an accident inasmuch as it is at present subsisting in the subject which is the soul does not contradict its being a substance inasmuch as it is a quiddity which, if it existed externally, would not be in a subject. Rather the impossible is that a thing be substance and accident in the external world, meaning that its quiddity, if it existed externally, would be in a subject and not in a subject. Likewise, its being particular in terms of its subsistence in a particular soul does not contradict its universality in terms of its correspondence to many individuals, meaning that what is obtained in the intellect from each of them, upon abstraction from accidents, is that same form. Then the relation of acquisition to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence to the quiddity in the external world. So likewise[?], the quiddity has no actuality in the external world, and its accident called existence has another actuality such that they combine as the combination of recipient and received. Similarly, the form has no actuality in the intellect, and its accident called acquisition has another actuality. Rather the form in the intellect is the very quiddity which has actuality in the external world, and it has an accident which is acquisition. And if they coincide in truth, there is no priority or posteriority between them.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections concerning the nature of the mental form (al-ṣūrah al-ʿilmiyyah) and al-Taftazani's comprehensive answer. The objections are: (1) The mental form is an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in the soul, yet it is said to correspond to an external substance; but an accident cannot correspond to a substance, much less to its very quiddity. (2) The mental form is said to be universal, but as an accident inhering in a particular soul it must be particular. (3) Al-Taftazani sometimes defines knowledge as the acquisition of the form and sometimes as the form itself, an apparent inconsistency. Al-Taftazani answers by appealing to the principle of different considerations (iʿtibārāt). The same thing can be considered under different aspects without contradiction. For instance, the mental form is an accident when considered as subsisting in the soul (its mode of existence in the mind), but it corresponds to a substance when considered as a quiddity (māhīyah) that, if existing externally, would exist not in a subject. The impossibility only arises if the same thing, under the same consideration, is both substance and accident externally. Similarly, the form is particular in its existence (as an accident in a particular soul) but universal in its meaning (its correspondence to many individuals) after abstraction from accidents. Thus, the same form (intentionally) is obtained from each individual. Then al-Taftazani draws an analogy: the relation of mental acquisition (ḥuṣūl) to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence (wujūd) to the quiddity in the external world. Just as the quiddity has no actuality in external existence apart from its accident of existence—they combine as recipient and received—so too the form has no actuality in the intellect apart from its accident of acquisition. But crucially, the form in the intellect is the very quiddity that has actuality externally, with acquisition as an accident. And if they coincide in truth, there is no priority or posteriority between them; the form and its acquisition are simultaneous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental forms cannot correspond to external substances because they are accidents in the soul.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that a mental form is an accident in its mode of existence in the mind but represents a quiddity that would be a substance externally; different considerations remove the contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani resolve the objection that a mental form as an accident cannot correspond to an external substance?
A) He denies that mental forms are accidents
B) He argues that the form is considered an accident only in the mind and a substance in its external quiddity
C) He claims that substances can be accidents in the mind
D) He says the objection is based on a misunderstanding of correspondence

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends the correspondence of mental forms to external substances by distinguishing between the form's mental mode of existence and its quidditative nature, employing the logical principle of different considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in your faith or understanding, do you honestly search for a different consideration that might resolve it, or do you simply reject the truth? Examine your heart: is there an area where you have dismissed a truth because you refused to change your perspective?
?
Al-Taftazani teaches that the mental form in your intellect is the very quiddity of the thing known. When you claim to know God through His attributes, is your knowledge truly a real correspondence to His essence, or are you content with a vague mental image that you have never rigorously tested against revelation?
?
The acquisition of knowledge (ḥuṣūl) is distinct from the form itself. In your own learning, do you often mistake the process (reading, memorizing) for actual understanding? How might this confusion account for a lack of certainty and transformation in your life?

The Three Objections Against Mental Forms
Taftazani enumerates three objections against mental forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key objections raised against the theory that knowledge is a mental form.

Core Idea
Mental forms cannot be both accidents in the soul and universal representations of substances.

Before Taftazani presents his defense, he first lays out the strongest objections against the theory of mental forms. These objections challenge the coherence of claiming that an accident in the soul can correspond to an external substance, be universal, and be both the form and its acquisition. Understanding these objections is essential to appreciating the subtlety of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the three distinct objections.
Pay attention to the phrasing of each objection.
2
Identify the three objections
List the three objections: substance-accident mismatch, universality of accident, and duality of knowledge.
Consider why each objection seems powerful.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Notice that all objections assume mental forms must have the same properties as external objects.
This assumption is what the answer will challenge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاضر الذي لا تلتفت إليه النفس، مدركاً، وليس كذلك، ومنها أن الصورة العلمية عرض قائم بالنفس وقد جعلتموها مطابقة للموجود العيني الذي ربما يكون جوهراً، بل نفس ماهيته، وامتناع كون العرض مطابقاً للجوهر، ونفس ماهيته معلوم بالضرورة وأيضاً: جعلتموها كلية مع أن كون العرض القائم بالنفس الجزئية جزئياً ضروري وأيضاً تجعلون العلم تارة حصول الصورة، وتارة نفس الصورة مع ظهور الفرق بينهما.
والجواب: أن الممتنع هو كون الشيء الواحد باعتبار واحد جوهراً وعرضاً، أو كلياً وجزئياً، وأما عند اختلاف الاعتبار فلا، فإن كون الصورة العقلية عرضاً من حيث كونها في الحال قائمة بالموضوع الذي هو النفس لا ينافي كونها جوهراً من حيث إنها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع، وإنما المستحيل كون الشيء جوهراً وعرضاً في الخارج بمعنى كونها ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع ولا في موضوع، وكذا كونها جزئية من حيث قيامها بالنفس الجزئية لا ينافي كليتها من حيث مطابقتها للأفراد الكثيرة، بمعنى أن الحاصل في العقل من كل منها عند التجريد عن العوارض تكون تلك الصورة بعينها، ثم نسبة الحصول إلى الصورة في العقل نسبة الوجود إلى الماهية في الخارج، فكماله ليس للماهية تحقق في الخارج، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كذلك ليس للصورة تحقق في العقل، ولعارضها المسمى بالحصول تحقق آخر، وإنما الزيادة بمعنى أن المفهوم من هذا غير المفهوم من ذاك، فبهذا الاعتبار يصح جعل العلم تارة نفس الصورة، وتارة حصولها.
And among them is that the mental form is an accident subsisting in the soul, and you have made it corresponding to the external existent which may be a substance, indeed the very quiddity of it, and the impossibility of an accident corresponding to a substance, and the very quiddity of it is known necessarily. Also: you have made it universal while that an accident subsisting in the particular soul is necessarily particular. Also you sometimes make knowledge the acquisition of the form, and sometimes the form itself, despite the apparent difference between them. The answer: That what is impossible is for one single thing in one respect to be substance and accident, or universal and particular; but when the respect differs, it is not. For the mental form being an accident in respect of its subsistence in the subject which is the soul does not preclude it from being a substance in respect of its being a quiddity which, if it existed externally, would not be in a subject. The impossible is only for a thing to be substance and accident in the external world in the sense that its quiddity, if it existed externally, would both be in a subject and not in a subject. Similarly, its being particular in respect of its subsistence in the particular soul does not preclude its universality in respect of its correspondence to many individuals, meaning that what is obtained in the intellect from each of them upon abstraction from accidents is that same form. Then the relation of acquisition to the form in the intellect is like the relation of existence to quiddity in the external world. For just as quiddity has no realization in the external world, and its accident called existence has another realization, such that the former does not combine the way the recipient and the received combine, so too the form has no realization in the intellect, and its accident called acquisition has another realization; the addition is only that the concept of this is other than the concept of that. In this respect it is valid to make knowledge sometimes the form itself and sometimes its acquisition.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the theory that knowledge is a mental form, followed by a detailed answer. The objections are: (1) How can a mental accident correspond to an external substance? (2) How can an accident in the particular soul be universal? (3) How can knowledge be both the form and its acquisition? The answer explains that impossibility only arises when the same thing is considered in the same respect; different respects remove the contradiction. The mental form is an accident in its subsistence in the soul, but a substance in terms of its quiddity if it existed externally. Similarly, it is particular as subsisting in a particular soul, but universal in that the same abstracted form corresponds to many individuals. The relation of acquisition to the form is like existence to quiddity: just as quiddity and existence are distinct concepts but not separate entities, acquisition and the form are distinct concepts but not separate realities. Thus it is permissible to call knowledge the form or its acquisition depending on the respect.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the objections raised against mental forms?
A) Mental forms are accidents but correspond to substances.
B) Mental forms are universal despite being accidents in a particular soul.
C) Mental forms are eternal and uncreated.
D) Knowledge is sometimes defined as the form and sometimes as its acquisition.

Key Takeaway
The three objections challenge the consistency of mental forms but rely on confusing the respects in which properties are attributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you 'know' something, do you treat your mental image of it as the thing itself? How does the first objection—that an accident cannot correspond to a substance—expose a potential deception in your everyday confidence about what you know?
?
The third objection points to a sloppy conflation: knowledge is both the acquisition of a form and the form itself. In your study of ‘aqeedah, are there concepts you have blurred together to avoid the hard work of precise distinction? Will you commit to clarity even when it is uncomfortable?
?
You claim to know universal truths—like the oneness of Allah. But the second objection says every form in your particular soul must be particular. How can you be certain that your knowledge of tawḥīd is not merely a particular mental accident? What makes it truly universal?
